As our audience surely knows, the United States is now enforcing a blockade on maritime traffic to and from Iranian ports, which has triggered another spike in oil prices. US Centcom said this week that the blockade has quote "completely halted economic trade to and from Iranian ports by sea." So Scott, as we move past the 50-day mark since the start of the conflict, where do things stand today from a trade and economic standpoint? Well, it's almost impossible to put a pin in this because the situation has moved so rapidly. The pace and tempo of operations is remarkable. I would note that I have taken to considering positions taken by Iran and statements by the Iranian government as sort of a split screen. In fact, there's three panels to the split screen. There are the Mullahs, who haven't said much since the opening implosion of the leadership when essentially the strikes decapitated, interrupted the brunch, I guess is the way to say it, and most of the leadership including the religious leadership that was present wound up dead. So it's hard to say what their position is but certainly if you look at the Iranian constitution it's like two parts: there's a religious part and a secular part. So those are two of the three screens available. So you have the Mullahs, you have the citizen or the conventional government, and they appear normal but don't have much power. That's in a normal situation, but they're the people who get sent to the negotiations to talk, but they don't seem to be able to accomplish much. The third group is the Islamic Republican Guard Corps, the IRGC, who operates as basically the organized crime faction of the government. And they're quite independent, they're independent in their financing and in their command structure from the other two governments, whatever they are. So it's very hard to sort out. That's as close as I can get personally to it. And as I look at this, you're seeing those three different interest groups, three different parties play out. One of the hard parts of an endgame, whenever that comes about, will be to figure out who there is to make peace with or to settle this conflict with. Who's in charge? And so that aside, we've seen a lot of claims and counter-claims about the Strait of Hormuz and what effect that has. And volatility in particularly energy markets has been massive since then. There've been a number of steps taken, including lifting of sanctions temporarily. But overall, energy prices are high and volatile. However, if you think just eight days ago, after 38 days of combat operations, the Strait was closing, but that was also the start of the ceasefire. And that was a point in time when basically the negotiations team from the US was led by Vice President Vance. He eventually walked out, sounded similar to Reykjavik with Reagan and Gorbachev in some ways. But what it did was trigger a full blockade of Iranian ports that you mentioned by the US Navy. What we realized in that week between April 8th when the ceasefire started and today is that probably Iran never really had control of the Strait, they never were in a position to close it. Closest control was the maritime insurance companies. But they weren't able to enforce their conditions no matter what. And the US blockade is vitally important from a strategic standpoint. In my mind, if you're going to go to a war with a petrostate, you've got to cut off the energy revenue. Unless you do that, they continue to be able to rearm and redeploy and pay troops and everything else. But I think that was the dramatic effect that the US blockade is having. Now, there's much more to come. You know, you don't have two Marine Expeditionary Forces and the 82nd Airborne in-theater to enforce a blockade. Okay? They do other things. And so my sense is something's more to come, but at least that's a way to cut through a little of the fog. Yeah, and Bill I want to get your thoughts, of course we're recording this on Thursday the 16th and anything could happen between now and when this is released on Monday the 20th. But Bill where are you on this? I'm really tempted to ask Scott whether every government has an organized crime faction or not, but that would be impertinent in the present circumstances. I had one short-term observation and then I think several sort of longer-term consequences of this regardless of the immediate end of it that we're going to have to deal with for I think years to come. The short-term thing is it's a reminder, my column next week is going to deal with this too, it's the first rule of holes is that if you're in one, stop digging. And Trump is in one here and he's not stopping digging. And I don't see a near-term solution to what he's trying to do. I think his thinking probably was that, you know, look what we did in Venezuela. You know, we decapitated them, took the country over and everything is fine and we did it overnight. And so we'll do it again with Iran. And that turned out to be a miscalculation for a variety of reasons. And it also, I think, revealed that they don't really seem to have a Plan B for a lot of this stuff. I mean, there were no shortage of people that were telling them beforehand: if you do this, the Iranians are going to interdict shipping in the Strait and they're going to use all these economic weapons because that's what they got. And we rolled ahead anyway, and now they're doing exactly what people predicted they were going to do. And I think we were caught kind of by surprise. We'll see. And now we're maybe getting organized, but we are still in one of these situations where it's not over. One guy earlier this week characterized this as a state of "no war, no peace, and no oil." And that's likely to continue, I think, longer than anybody expected, with the resulting economic consequences, which is going to be more inflation and over the longer term shortages to the extent that factories have to close or cut back production because they don't have the energy to keep them running or because they can't get the parts and components that they need either because they're locked up in the Persian Gulf or because there are, again, fuel shortages that are constraining shipping from other ports. So this is going to last for a while. The longer-term consequences are, I think, three. One, this is a reminder of what we've said on this podcast frequently, which is everything is connected. And you do these things and then there are always unexpected downstream consequences. And you discover dependencies that you didn't know existed or didn't pay any attention to, and all of a sudden it's a big issue because you didn't see it coming. So we're going through that right now, and competent governments prepare for that, we review every contingency and have a plan for every possible outcome. And it was apparent in this case that we haven't. The second one is I think it's really ironic that Donald Trump, the apostle of fossil fuels, is now engaged in an effort that's driving everybody away from them. Because what he's demonstrating is that to a lot of countries, particularly in Asia, if you're dependent on fossil fuels, you know, you're vulnerable. Because what the Iranians have done is weaponized that particular item, and they've done it so far fairly effectively. And a blockade, which might be the appropriate military tactic, is only going to make things worse from an energy standpoint because what we're doing is turning back Iranian ships, 13 I think in the last couple days now, the latest count, who, some of which I think were probably carrying oil. So what you are seeing globally, I mean less here and more everywhere else is: let's start thinking about alternative sources of energy. Let's start thinking about electricity, let's start thinking more about batteries, about EVs, about wind, about solar, all the things Trump hates. What he's doing is driving everybody, no pun intended, driving everybody in that direction, including here. I mean the market is going to make these decisions, not the government. We'll see what people choose to do. But I would expect, you know, an increase in demand for solar panels, more wind projects if they can figure out a way to get around Trump's efforts to get rid of them, greater demand for EVs and a lot more R&D on batteries and battery storage, which is exactly where we were going anyway pre-Trump. It's hard to tell the long-term consequences this close in, but just a couple of observations. First, West Texas Intermediate is at $92 a barrel today. That's not an unheard-of price, it's not even a particularly high price in the last six weeks. And we are exporting oil now, filling up a lot of tankers here that can't fill up in the Gulf. So you may be right about this in the long run, although I think the more likely conversion option would be the gasification projects. I mean if you look at what the Luftwaffe flew their planes with, fueled their planes in 1944, it was coal converted, gasified into avgas. That's how South Africa avoided sanctions in the '70s. So there's technology out there that will be pursued as well. No one is going to be without hydrocarbons forever. But here's the thing, there's a lot of ways this could go. But importantly, I think it's, you know, either there's no plan and this is just chaos, or there's a plan that we may not be privy to. Okay? I don't think we got to where we are now, which looks pretty strong position militarily and we're using the ceasefire to refresh the target list, I'm certain, and that control of Iranian ports, US control, is closer to stabilizing shipping in the Gulf than it is disrupting it. So I think that there's a plan that we might not be privy to. Let's watch it work, let's see what's happening before we project a lot of long-term consequences. Well, we'll see what's happening. I'm more pessimistic than you are on this, as usual. And I think that we're seeing some gasification project investments being cut back now in anticipation of more limited demand. So we'll see. The last consequence, also long-term, is I think this has revealed the fragility of the Gulf states. The UAE, Bahrain, Qatar, Kuwait have spent decades telling the reliable stable governments, you know, "fly your airplanes there, use us as a hub, invest here, do business here, we're safe and reliable." And through no fault of their own, I think that's been exposed as not true. Oh, I think it's just the contrary, Bill. I think the Abraham Accords were just a starting point of a different vision for the greater Middle East which is based on commerce and cooperation, which those states are practicing now, and they want us to finish the job with Iran. Okay? Iran's had the other way, which is, you know, let's use violence and proxy groups and everything to create disorder and get our way that way. So I think the fact that the alliance with the Gulf states has held is evidence that there's something different going on in the greater Middle East. So once again, I'm less skeptical there than you are. Well, the question will be what investors do. Yes. That is exactly it. And the extent to which they pull their money out, they go somewhere else, look for other air transportation hubs instead of Dubai. Maybe I'm wrong on this, but I think that people are reading the tea leaves in a more pessimistic way right now. But we'll see. To my mind, the Gulf states are behaving better than some of our so-called NATO allies. We got to sort of straighten out who our friends are here at some point. You know, but if you look at the post-World War II, well even pre-World War II history of the Middle East, you know, these conflicts go back, many of them go back millennia. Definitely. And nobody ever wins. I mean, in the short run somebody might win. But the people who lose go underground and then they become terrorists and sort of non-state actors and they chip away at your credibility for the next 20 years until the next war breaks out. So you know, the Gulf states may say "finish the job in Iran." My point is we're not going to be able to finish the job in Iran. Yeah, what does that mean? Because there's always going to be people there that they'll go underground and they'll be back. That's the nature of the territory. That famous book of the history of the modern Middle East, the title was "The Peace to End All Peace." It's an excellent book, by the way, I forget the author but I've given away more copies than I can count. But you're right, that's the essential character of that part of the world at this point. Well, we could talk about this for hours, but we must move on and I'm going to make a hard pivot here to talk about... talk about trade on The Trade Guys? You gotta be kidding. Talk about trade on The Trade Guys. We must. Let's turn to the administration's use of section 122. On April 10th, the US Court of International Trade heard arguments in the lawsuit filed by 24 states challenging the administration's use of 122 to impose 10% tariffs on most imports, which they argued was only an attempt to work around the Supreme Court's IEEPA decision. Scott, what are the arguments here on both sides of the case? Well, they may be correct on motives but that doesn't really matter. There has been a long and persistent current account deficit, which turned into a trade deficit over the years, which tariffs would help correct in the medium term. And those large imbalances almost always correct themselves over time. That said, we do have trade deficits now, but we don't have a balance of payments crisis. And the difference between when that law was written in 1974 and now are floating exchange rates. Because of floating exchange rates, you don't actually get into the balance of payments crisis that we were facing in 1971 when a French military vessel, naval vessel, showed up in New York harbor asking for the gold in Fort Knox to be delivered to the ship so it could take it back to France where they wanted it to be, and wound up with President Nixon closing the gold window, which was sort of easier than IEEPA. It was basically send a note, handwritten note to the Treasury Secretary John Connally asking him to temporarily close the gold window and temporary's still going on, it never reopened. But once the gold window was closed and we no longer exchanged foreign currency for gold at a fixed exchange rate, we were able to work our way out of the balance of payments crisis that the country was facing. One would presume Congress wrote this particular section of the Trade Act of 1974 with that incident in mind. But whether it did or not, the statute has not been utilized and therefore there's not really much interpretation, there's not much oversight being conducted on it. So lawyers on both sides are free to speculate. Now, what I think is going to happen here is that the administration is going to be able to use it, they're using it at the 10% level, and they'll continue to use it till the authority expires in 150 days when they'll move to other things. You know look, tariffs before President Trump took office were around 2.5%. Now that's the broadest possible measure of tariffs, that's total tariff revenue divided by total merchandise trade imports. That's the biggest look you can have. Went from 2.5% before the Trump began in his second term to roughly 13% now, 12 or 13% now, which is a big increase but one that seems to have stabilized. And this is the first move at keeping them there. So regardless of motives, I think they'll probably get their 150 days. Yeah, Bill what stood out to you from the hearing? Well, I do have to point out that I'm so old that I did work on IEEPA a little bit. I did not work on the Trade Act of 1974. So I can't provide personal insight. I'm old but not that old. Well I was on the hill at the time, but my boss was not involved in that particular thing. As a very young aide, I'm sure. In my 20s, yes. Very young. Scott I think has the history right. For those who don't remember the history, the United States up until Nixon was on the gold standard, which means basically that we promised to redeem paper certificates in gold on demand or silver. And if you go back to before Roosevelt, you had banknotes that said that: promise to pay to the bearer on demand one dollar, ten dollars, whatever it was in gold or in silver. I still have some what are called silver certificates, which were at the time one dollar bills which you could actually, in theory anyway, go to a bank and demand a silver dollar. That of course creates problems for countries because if there is a huge bump of imports where we're paying a lot of money out, what happens when the foreign holders of all those US dollars, like the French ship, come in and say "give us our gold"? And there's a real possibility that you're going to run out. That is a balance of payments crisis. And Nixon took us off the gold standard in order to forestall that. And Congress reacted exactly as Scott said. The current argument is that given that history and given the fact that we are now off the gold standard and exchange rates are flexible, that situation cannot repeat itself. I'm not so sure about that because there are other countries, developing countries, that have had balance of payments crises even though their currency floats. But one of the arguments is, I think there's basically two: one, we could not have this kind of crisis in the current monetary system that the world has. The second argument, which is probably more compelling, is whether you could have one or not, we don't have one. We're not in a crisis. We're not even in a balance of trade crisis, one could argue, since we've had a balance of trade deficit for 75 years, it's hard to say that this is a crisis. Balance of payments includes more than trade. It includes basically services in addition to the goods where we have a surplus and it includes transfers of money back and forth in a debit-credit kind of way. The money that when we export something, money comes into the United States, that's a credit in the current account. Just like when we buy something from overseas, money goes out, that's a debit in the current account. But if somebody comes to the United States and buys a treasury bond from outside the country, that's an inflow into our treasury, and that counts in the current account, doesn't count in the trade balance. So we'll see. The judges hearing this case are not the same as the ones that heard the IEEPA case. There's three different ones. It was not clear from the oral arguments which way they're leaning. I think a lot of the discussion was, you know, what did Congress mean when they passed this? And unfortunately there aren't that many congressmen around who were there when they passed it to advise them. So it's hard to predict what they might do. I do think that this court, which is the lowest court on this issue, will probably act fairly quickly. They did in IEEPA. But then the appellate process, and there surely will be one no matter who wins, I think will pass 150 days. So it really is the important issue here, which is our third topic is going to be: what is the administration doing to get ready for July 24th when the 10% expires? Yeah, just for context, how quickly was the IEEPA decision handed down from this court? I think it was about three weeks. Scott, do you remember? I think that's right. Okay. It was a fairly expedited arguments and proceeding. People were surprised, actually. And you know, it was faster than expected. Yeah. And maybe this one won't be. As I said, different judges. But a lot of people are thinking, you know, early May is not out of the question for this. Yeah. Well Bill, you teed up our final discussion topic today and we're going to check in on the administration's plans for applying tariffs through section 301. Last week Secretary Bessent said at an event quote: "We had it set back at the Supreme Court, but we will be implementing or conducting 301 studies so that tariffs could be back in place at the previous level by the beginning of July." So Bill, what's going on here and does that timeline sound right to you? I think that's what they intend. Yes. I think the game, they've had Plan B for a long time. I wouldn't say they knew they were going to lose, but this is a case where they realized they could lose and actually developed Plan B. And Plan B is let's figure out what laws we can use essentially to reimpose the same tariffs. And the 10% is a stopgap because it's temporary and it's limited to 15% and they've only gone to 10 and Trump said they were going to go to 15, he hasn't done that, I don't think he will for a variety of reasons. We can avoid the weeds on that one. But their new tool of choice is section 301, which allows the US Trade Representative technically as a matter of law to take action against countries that engage in unjustifiable, unreasonable practices that burden or restrict United States commerce. I mean everybody knows he's going to do what the President tells him, but technically it's a USTR decision, which is important because it means it's subject to the administrative procedures act, which is why they're receiving public comments. That period expired yesterday on the 15th, and why they're going to hold public hearings on what to do about this, which will be in the first week of May. What they did is announce 76 investigations. 16 involving alleged overcapacity, production overcapacity by different countries, and 60 alleging either engaging in forced labor or more likely not doing enough to prevent forced labor and allowing imports made with forced labor to come into your country. A number of countries are subject to both investigations. But technically there's 76 of them. And there will be lawsuits on this too. People are already getting ready, you can be sure of that. Although Secretary Bessent you know said this week that they he feels these are more legally durable. I don't know if you agree with that or not. Well, they are more legally durable in the sense unlike IEEPA, 301 does have the word tariff in it. You know, and it is a remedy that the President actually can or the USTR actually can impose. What the other side will say, the plaintiffs in whatever lawsuits are filed, will say primarily is: this is all a scam. None of these investigations are real. The outcome is precooked. Everybody's going to be guilty. And there really isn't any investigation here. And the court will have to decide what it thinks about that, whether it's true or not, and B, even if it's true, whether it makes any difference. Because the statute is pretty broad. My prediction about what will happen, this is a fearless prediction, is that everybody will be guilty and the remedy will be reinstatement of the trade agreements that were negotiated last year. Because if you look at the legal aspects of this, the basis for those agreements was IEEPA. And that's been thrown out. So the only basis for at least the tariff part of the agreement right now is section 122. That expires July 24th. If they want to maintain the agreements, they need to come up with another legal basis for them. 301 would be that. And 301 is perfect. It is perfect for that. Because there are unfair practices everywhere. So you get your trading partner to confess to an unfair practice, and the penalty is signing the trade agreement, then the statute underlies the tariffs as applied and everybody goes home. Exactly. And Justice Kavanaugh's dissent predicted it perfectly. The flaw in the argument is that while everybody is guilty of something, I'm not sure that you could convince the courts that all 60 of them are guilty of forced labor violations or that all 16 of them are guilty of overcapacity. They're probably guilty of something else. And maybe the investigations will end up being broader. Unfair practices abound. Of course. We've been known to do that ourselves from time to time. Once in a while, yes. But the administration is going to be challenged to prove it's not a scam and that these are real investigations. And one of the things they'll have to contend with is on the forced labor side for example, if you're going to decide for example that Malaysia, Indonesia and Vietnam have all not been sufficiently tough on forced labor, then you're going to go on and say the remedy for that is an 18% tariff in Malaysia, a 20% tariff on Vietnam and a 19% tariff on Indonesia. So the remedy is different in all three cases for exactly the same offense. And they're going to have to explain that, which has led some people to think that they may not reimpose exactly the agreements from last year. There may be some tweaking in order to resolve questions like that that make it more difficult for the plaintiffs to say: this is just a joke. You know, you cook this up, you decide all these cases in advance, it's not a real investigation, and you're just trying to reimpose the trade agreements, which I think is probably true. And this is an opportunity to clean things up. But I think that there's sufficient flexibility. I mean look, our trading partners always hated section 301. They tried to get rid of it in trade negotiations every time it came up, and they thought they'd managed to do it but didn't. But in any case, you know, section 301 has been limited to two or three cases a year over the 50-plus years it's existed. Now to all of a sudden have all these cases, it looks suspicious. They'll have to do something to make it look more legitimate. I think you'll find this interview a lot of fun. At the end, I'll come back to talk about lessons learned. Hi, this is Steve Eisman, and welcome to another episode of The Real Eisman Playbook. You know, sometimes I think about how to categorize the guests that we have on our show. There's one way to categorize it: two types of guests, time-specific and not time-specific. We often have guest stock analysts talking about what's going on in earnings season, or guests talking about what's going on politically. We had a guest a few weeks ago talking about what's going on in the war. That's time-specific. But then we often have authors of books where it wouldn't matter what time of year we show this interview because it would be good at any time. Today is one of those interviews. Today, my guest is Jeffrey Hirsch, who is the author of the annual book, Stock Trader's Almanac. Here's my copy, the 2026 version. Jeffrey, welcome to The Real Eisman Playbook. Great to be with you, Steve. So, give us a little history of this book, and then we'll move on from there. Sure. Well, my illustrious father, who Steve Forbes called "the legend," was the man who started the book back in 1966, the year I was born. He had been working at a place called Indicator Digest, which was a newsletter analyst shop out of Palisades Park, New Jersey. It was a sort of proving grounds for analysts and writers. He had started working there with his cousin, my Uncle Sam, who was a songwriter. He wrote "Cocktails for Two" and "My Old Flame," and had sold his publishing company to RCA years ago. The stock dropped, you know, and he got stock for his publishing company, so he became a student of the market. Then he ran an old ad, an AB split ad, back in 1961 in Barron's with two different headlines, and the ad hit. He calls up my father, who was working in the music industry, and says, "Yale, I want you to come in and run operations." Your father's name was Yale? Yale Hirsch. It's a good name. It's a good name. Great name. He was a songwriter. At one point, I had—your father was a songwriter, too? Composer, piano player. There was a lot of music in my house. You know, I turned him on to some rock and roll over the years, but he turned me on to a lot of music. So he came in, and I think, honestly, the music is part of the pattern recognition and the understanding of the rhythm of the market. Well, music and math have a tremendous relationship. Tremendous, and he was a very large-brained individual. He could turn a phrase. I mean, some of his famous phrases include: "If Santa Claus should fail to call, bears may come to Broad and Wall." I mean, this is all Yale's songwriting ability. He could write a headline as well as ad copy. In '61, he goes and starts working with Sam Coslow, who was my godfather. After a few years there, he wanted to take all these indicators and market patterns and put them all together in a calendar format so that he could follow the market schedule along with his own. He went out on his own while my mom was pregnant with me. You know, I was in utero there, and he started his own business in '66 with the Stock Trader's Almanac. He spent the next 18 months building it, put out the '68 edition in the fall of '67, and it hit. Everybody on Wall Street dug it. It's going to be 60 years old soon. The 60th edition is coming out for '27, so it must be doing something right. It seems to continue to resonate with people. It's basically watching how the market schedule correlates with the year, the season, the institutional quarterly patterns, and the daily patterns. What's the premise? This book has a... it's not the longest book in the world. No, I mean, it's not even 200 pages long. It's filled with numbers and graphs. It's a very information-rich book, but I felt as I was reading it that there is an underlying premise. What is it? It's giving yourself the calendar edge. I mean, it's tracking—it’s the real behavioral finance book. This is how people behave on a regular basis, how institutions behave on a daily pattern, weekly pattern, monthly pattern, annual pattern, quarterly pattern, and the four-year presidential cycle pattern. It focuses on repetitive human behavior. As humans, we are creatures of habit. We do things repeatedly and habitually with our money, just like we do with getting up every day around the same time, having our week the same way, paying our bills at the same time, and going on vacations at the same time of year. There's Triple Witching, there's holidays. I mean, we've got right here the Passover and Easter holidays. This has a certain seasonal behavior to it as well. It's spring, so things happen. During the summer doldrums, you have more daylight in the Northern hemisphere and people are doing other things away from the trading desk. They're out playing golf and going on vacations and the kids are home from school, so there's a general flow to what people do. Granted, there are exogenous events and current events—the Middle East is a perfect example. But generally... Well, your book did not predict the war in Iran. No. But nobody else did either. Right. Somebody asked me on my members webinar yesterday, "What black swan events do you foresee?" I chuckled. I bet that's what a black swan event is—the things you don't foresee. I'd be Nostradamus if I did. No, so let's start with and work through a bunch of different patterns that are in this book. We'll go through some of the more important ones. At the beginning of the book, you talk about a midterm election pattern. Yes. And as everybody knows, there's a midterm election in November. What will happen? We have no idea, because at the rate things are going, the world will flip 15 different ways between now and November. Making a prediction about the midterm election is ridiculous. But you have a bunch of stuff in here about a midterm election pattern. Yeah, talk about that. Another one of Yale's phrases was calling the midterm year the "bottom-picker's paradise." Okay. In the last 19 cycles, 11 of the last 16 bear markets bottomed in the midterm year. This is a time when incumbent presidents generally lose House seats and their party generally loses seats. It's very historically common. We see the weakest period. What we call the "weak spot" of the four-year cycle is Q2 and Q3 of the midterm year. This is where the campaigning heats up and starts to take attention away from the market and the economy. The market tends to go sideways and chop. It's right in there with our worst six months of the year as well. Then you see the best year of the four-year cycle has been the pre-election year, so you've got this bottoming tendency. We had a nice textbook October midterm year bottom in 2022. I have a page in the book that shows all these different things that happen in the midterm year, and I've highlighted all these conflicts. I mean, Russia invades Ukraine. We had 2018 where there were issues with the Fed. There's things that happen in the midterm year as the political arena is changing. It tends to leave the market and the economy vulnerable to different things happening out there. So we've got this midterm bottom-picking situation. The gain from the midterm low to the pre-election year high is 46.3% for the Dow, on average, going back to 1914. And when does it bottom? I mean, right now, we're as choppy as choppy can be. Usually Q2 or Q3, in the latter part. So late summer? Late summer, we see a bunch in October. There are a few March ones in there that I noticed recently. What my father called the Best Six Months switching strategy was introduced in the 1987 Almanac, and 1986. Basically, you know, it's the flip side of "sell in May and go away." By the way, do you believe in "sell in May and go away"? No, you don't? I mean, I don't disbelieve in it. We don't go away. We take some profits and we reposition. But the granddaddy of all the seasonal patterns is the best six months switching strategy. Okay, so what is that? The raw version is you're buying Dow, S&P, or NASDAQ on October 30th or 31st, the last trading day of the month, and selling April 30th. We use a MACD technical timing indicator. What does that mean? MACD is again, written and invented by Jerry Appel, the late Jerry Appel, which is Moving Average Convergence Divergence. It takes a couple of exponential moving averages, and it's a momentum shifting indicator. It's pretty common in technical analysis. Not my strong suit, but... You should know that. I gathered, but the thing that Jerry Appel, who I knew as a kid, wrote when he created this MACD indicator was that it's not something you use in a vacuum. You don't use it by itself. You use it in conjunction with another reason to buy or sell something. For us, it's seasonality. For you, it could be a fundamental analysis of a stock or a sector. You use the MACD to sort of confirm an entry or exit. Okay. So that improves the Best Six Months. So it's showing that there's momentum in a stock? Or in an index. Or the breakdown of momentum. There's a gentleman named David Aronson, a technical analyst guy with Baruch College and the CMT, the Market Technicians. He wrote a book called "Evidence-Based Technical Analysis." He took 6,200 and change different blackbox systems and ran them through the scientific method. He was disproving, trying to disprove the null hypothesis that all of these blackbox systems had no predictive power and all the results would be chance. They all failed. They failed his test. I mean, we do like the "sell half on a double," or if you're up a bunch, take some profits. We have different strategies for different positions. But the granddaddy of all the seasonal patterns is the Best Six Months switching strategy. Okay, so what is that? The raw version is you're buying, you know, Dow, S&P, NASDAQ, on October 30th and 31st, or the last trading day of the month, and selling April 30th. We use a MACD technical timing indicator to—what does that mean? MACD is again, another Jerry, written—invented by Jerry Appel, the late Jerry Appel, which is Moving Average Convergence Divergence. It takes a couple of exponential moving averages, and it's a momentum shifting indicator. So, it's pretty common in technical analysis. Not my strong suit, but what? You should know that. I gathered, but you know, the thing that Jerry Appel, who I knew as a kid, wrote when he created this MACD indicator was: it's not something you use in a vacuum. You don't use it by itself. You use it in conjunction with another reason to buy or sell something. For us, it's seasonality; for you, it could be, you know, a fundamental analysis of a stock or a sector or something. You use the MACD to sort of confirm an entry or exit. Okay. So that improves the Best Six Months. So it's showing that there's momentum in a stock, or in an index, or the breakdown of momentum. Okay, the breakdown of momentum. So, you know, we could get wonky and technical on that. I don't know if we want to do that, but the thing that we did back in 2008... there's a gentleman named David Aronson, a technical analyst guy with Baruch College and the CMT, you know, the Market Technicians. He wrote a book called Evidence-Based Technical Analysis. He took 6,200 and change different blackbox systems and ran them through the scientific method, disproving—trying to disprove—the null hypothesis that all of these blackbox systems had, you know, no predictive power, and all the result would be chance. They all failed. They, they, they all failed; they failed his test. They were not predictive. Correct. Okay. When we picked that book, back when my father was alive—and I think it was '08, if memory serves—as our best investment book for the Almanac, we asked him to take the Best Six Months switching strategy and put it through the same paces. Okay? He took it, starting in '87, because that was the first full year after we came out with it, so it wasn't back-tested. And long story short, lo and behold, unlike any of the other 6,000-plus different blackbox systems, it was the only one that had predictive power and was not the result of chance. Okay. He took 6,200 and change different blackbox systems and ran them through the scientific method, disproving—trying to disprove—the null hypothesis that all of these blackbox systems had, you know, no predictive power, and all the result would be chance. They all failed. They, they, they all failed; they failed his test. They were not predictive. Correct. Okay. When we picked that book, back when my father was alive—and I think it was '08, if memory serves—as our best investment book for the Almanac, we asked him to take the Best Six Months switching strategy and put it through the same paces. Okay? He took it, starting in '87, because that was the first full year after we came out with it, so it wasn't back-tested. And long story short, lo and behold, unlike any of the other 6,000-plus different blackbox systems, it was the only one that had predictive power and was not the result of chance. Okay. I I I I gathered, but you know, the the thing that Jerry Appel, who I knew as a kid, wrote when he created this this MACD indicator was, it's not something you use in a vacuum, you don't use it by itself. You use it in conjunction with another reason to buy or sell something. For us, it's seasonality, for you, it could be, you know, a fundamental analysis of a stock or a sector or something. You use the MACD to sort of confirm an entry or exit. Okay. So that improves the best six months. So it's showing that there's momentum in a stock. Or, or in an index. Watch CNBC's full interview with SEC Chairman Paul Atkins

CNBC Television(3.4M)·12:47·5.6K views·2026-04-20
Paul AtkinsChairman, SEC
TL;DRSEC Chairman Paul Atkins is shifting the agency away from "regulation by enforcement" toward a growth-oriented "ACT" strategy (Advance, Clarify, Transform) designed to lure innovative firms back to the U.S.
and remove regulatory barriers to revitalize the public IPO...

TL;DR

SEC Chairman Paul Atkins is shifting the agency away from "regulation by enforcement" toward a growth-oriented "ACT" strategy (Advance, Clarify, Transform) designed to lure innovative firms back to the U.S. and remove regulatory barriers to revitalize the public IPO market.

The "ACT" Strategy and Regulatory Pivot

Chairman Atkins highlighted a fundamental shift in the SEC’s philosophy, moving away from what he described as the "opaqueness" of the previous administration.

  • Pivoting from Enforcement: The agency is moving away from "regulation through enforcement," particularly in the crypto sector, to provide clearer guidelines for market participants.
  • The ACT Framework:
      • Advance: Modernizing the SEC's approach to embrace new technologies rather than "fending them off," with the goal of bringing offshore business back to the U.S.
      • Clarify: Increasing transparency through initiatives like the interpretative release with the CFTC, which defines the difference between tokenized securities and digital commodities.
      • Transform: Updating the SEC rulebook to make it "fit for purpose" for the modern economy.

Revitalizing the IPO Market

Atkins expressed a commitment to "making IPOs great again," noting that the number of public companies has halved over the last 30 years.

  • The Private-to-Public Gap: Companies are staying private much longer; what used to be an "Initial Public Offering" is now occurring at Series C or D funding rounds.
  • Barriers to Entry: Atkins identified three primary impediments keeping companies private:
      • Compliance Costs: The extreme complexity and total cost of the current disclosure system.
      • Vexatious Litigation: Uncertainty regarding whether the SEC will permit mandatory arbitration or fee-shifting to prevent meritless lawsuits.
      • Politicized Governance: The "weaponization" of corporate governance by activist shareholders pursuing political agendas.
  • The Liquidity Need: He noted that massive private companies (e.g., SpaceX, OpenAI) eventually need public markets to provide true daily valuations and liquidity for employees.

Market Integrity and Political Trading

The Chairman addressed concerns regarding market manipulation and the influence of political figures on market movements.

  • Washington Influence: Atkins noted that "disturbing" instances of market-moving comments—and subsequent suspicious trading activity—are being examined.
  • Agency Collaboration: The SEC is working closely with the Department of Justice (under US Attorney Jay Clayton) and the CFTC to monitor traceability and potential insider trading.
  • Prediction Markets:
      • Atkins deferred sports betting questions to the courts and the CFTC.
      • SEC Jurisdiction: The SEC focuses on "event contracts" that impinge on securities, such as binary options regarding whether a company will meet its quarterly earnings.

Private Credit and Retail Access

As private credit grows, the SEC is evaluating whether "normal" retail investors should have broader access to these traditionally opaque markets.

  • Semi-Liquid Instruments: The agency’s examination division is looking into disclosures for "interval funds" that use gates to limit withdrawals (often up to 5%).
  • Retirement Guardrails: The SEC is collaborating with the Treasury and Department of Labor on proposals to allow private market access for 401k plans.
  • Cautious Approach: Atkins emphasized that if retail access expands, "really strong guardrails" are mandatory because private markets remain significantly more opaque than public ones.

Notable Quotes

"We’ve pivoted from the old practice of regulation through enforcement and the opaqueness of the agency as it was... that was job one." — Paul Atkins
"We have half the number of public companies as we had 30 years ago. And so where did that go? That went into the private markets." — Paul Atkins
"I promised a new day at the SEC when I came aboard and we have." — Paul Atkins
It’s been nearly one year on the job. SEC chair Paul Atkins is leading a major shift in how the agency approaches regulation, crypto, and the broader markets. Joining us right now at the table, SEC Chairman Paul Atkins. Good morning to you. Good morning, Andrew. So, it’s been a year in the gig now. When you started and where you thought you’d be now, what do you think? Well, a year goes by quickly as you know, but we’ve made huge progress, I think. I promised a new day at the SEC when I came aboard, and we have. We’ve pivoted from the old practice of regulation through enforcement and the opaqueness of the agency as it was, for example with crypto, as you mentioned. That was job one, to focus on that. And so basically we’re instituting a strategy that I’m calling our ACT strategy: Advance, Clarify, and Transform. Advance meaning modernization of our approach, so rather than fending off new innovative types of technologies, we’re embracing them. And so we want to make them so that we’ll bring them from offshore, where people fled, to come back to the United States to really work on their products. And then second, to clarify things. SEC was very opaque as to how it would approach crypto and other things. We’ve clarified it with the CFTC. We had an interpretative release focusing on defining tokenized securities versus other digital assets that are commodities. And finally to transform, to focus on making our rulebook fit for purpose so that we can then make IPOs great again and enhance our public markets. When you look at what’s happened over the last 30 years, we have half the number of public companies as we had 30 years ago. And so where did that go? That went into the private markets. And so now people go public at C and D rounds after that, and they’re huge companies. Secondly is the vexatious litigation. In the past, the SEC has been very opaque as to what it would allow as far as public companies, companies going public, with respect to whether or not mandatory arbitration would be permitted or fee shifting. And then finally the weaponization of corporate governance by politicized shareholder activists adds another element. So those three things really stand as impediments, I think, for people going public. Merz, Lagarde & Deutsche Bank CEO Speak in Berlin at BdB 75th Anniversary Banking Summit | AC15

DWS News(284.0K)·66:36·138 views·2026-04-20
Friedrich MerzGerman Chancellor Christine LagardePresident, European Central Bank Christian SewingCEO, Deutsche Bank
TL;DREuropean leaders and banking executives are calling for an urgent deepening of the European Capital Markets Union and radical structural reforms in Germany to maintain global competitiveness amidst relentless geopolitical shocks and energy supply risks.
Competitiveness...

TL;DR

European leaders and banking executives are calling for an urgent deepening of the European Capital Markets Union and radical structural reforms in Germany to maintain global competitiveness amidst relentless geopolitical shocks and energy supply risks.

Germany’s Structural Reform Agenda

  • Competitiveness Deficit: Chancellor Friedrich Merz acknowledged that Germany's price competitiveness has eroded over years of "small steps" and requires a fundamental shift to remain a leading industrial site.
  • Fiscal & Social Reforms:
      • Social Security: A commitment to halt the rise of social insurance contributions. Merz noted that every 1% increase in contributions represents a €20 billion burden on the economy.
      • Healthcare: Plans for a structural reform of the statutory health insurance system to save €40 billion on the expenditure side.
      • Pensions: Moving toward a capital-funded pension pillar by 2027 to supplement the statutory system, reducing dependency on demographic shifts.
  • Economic Priorities: The government aims to lower energy costs, reduce corporate taxes, and aggressively cut bureaucracy, specifically targeting the "Gold-plating" of EU regulations where Germany implements stricter rules than required.

European Financial Sovereignty & The Single Market

  • Capital Markets Union (CMU): Deutsche Bank CEO Christian Sewing and Chancellor Merz both emphasized that Europe’s 450 million consumers are a massive untapped asset.
      • Equity vs. Debt: Merz advocated for a shift toward capital-market-oriented financing to improve corporate equity ratios and reduce dependence on bank loans.
      • Securitization: A call to revive the European securitization market to increase liquidity and financial infrastructure efficiency.
  • Banking Sector Consolidation: Merz expressed a nuanced view on mergers, stating that while large European banks are necessary, "hostile, aggressive takeovers" are unwelcome. He emphasized the importance of locally anchored small and medium-sized banks for the *Mittelstand*.
  • Savings and Investment: ECB President Christine Lagarde prioritized a "Savings and Investment Union" to ensure European capital stays within the continent to fund innovation and defense.

Global Geopolitical Shocks and Inflation Risks

  • The "Relentless" Environment: Lagarde characterized the current era by its overlapping crises: the pandemic, the war in Ukraine, the worst energy crisis in 50 years, and new military conflicts impacting the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Supply Chain Fragility:
      • Critical Inputs: Potential disruptions in the Strait of Hormuz threaten 13% of global oil consumption and critical materials like helium (semiconductors), methanol (chemicals), and fertilizers.
      • Food Security: Lagarde warned that fertilizer shortages could drive significant food inflation, which heavily influences broader public inflation expectations.
  • Energy Scenarios: The ECB is monitoring three paths—baseline, adverse, and severe. While energy prices haven't yet locked into the "adverse" scenario, the risk of shifting from price-driven inflation to output-hitting supply rationing remains high.

Monetary and Fiscal Policy Calibration

  • Data-Driven ECB: Lagarde confirmed the ECB has abandoned "forward guidance" in favor of a granular, data-driven approach. Policy will depend on two factors: the duration of current conflicts and the "second-round effects" of energy prices on wages and firm pricing.
  • Fiscal Discipline:
      • Selectivity: Lagarde warned that "fiscal space has shrunk" and governments cannot shield every household from every shock.
      • Support Structure: Future fiscal support must be temporary, targeted, and preserve price signals to encourage energy transition.
  • Transatlantic Relations: Merz highlighted the importance of maintaining a "reasonable personal relationship" with U.S. leadership (specifically mentioning Donald Trump) to preserve NATO and economic stability, regardless of political differences.
"The owl of Minerva begins its flight only as dusk falls... understanding comes after the experimentation, not before it. The truth is that these painful years have taught us a great deal already." — Christine Lagarde, ECB President
"Hostile, aggressive behavior [in takeovers] is something we decisively reject. We must look at what we together need in the European and German markets." — Friedrich Merz, German Chancellor
"If Europe does not take this path [of competitiveness], then Germany will also fail. No country in the EU is as dependent on the success of the internal market as we are." — Friedrich Merz, German Chancellor
"We have 450 million consumers... let's make sure that the massive savings that accumulate in your institutions are actually invested in this continent so we can be sovereign at home." — Christine Lagarde, ECB President

Sehr geehrter Bundeskanzler, Sie haben das Wort. – Sehr geehrter Herr Präsident, lieber Christian Sewing. Sehr geehrte Frau Präsidentin der Europäischen Zentralbank, liebe Christine Lagarde. Herr Hackenhoff, meine sehr geehrten Damen und Herren, Exzellenzen, liebe Kolleginnen und Kollegen aus dem Deutschen Bundestag. Herzlichen Dank für die freundliche Begrüßung und die Einladung zu Ihrer diesjährigen Jahrestagung. Ich möchte Ihnen zunächst im Namen der Bundesregierung, aber auch persönlich sehr herzlich zum 75. Geburtstag gratulieren. 75 ist kein Alter. Es ist kein Alter. Ganz im Gegenteil: Sie können auf eine 75-jährige Geschichte des Erfolges der deutschen privaten Banken zurückblicken – der großen und der kleinen, der internationalen und der regionalen. Sie ermöglichen seit 75 Jahren Investitionen, Sie finanzieren Unternehmen, Sie öffnen die Türen zum Kapitalmarkt. Und Sie sichern Arbeitsplätze. In Deutschland immerhin fast 540.000 Beschäftigte sind insgesamt in der deutschen Kreditwirtschaft tätig. Und Sie schaffen einen großen Mehrwert für unser Land, für unsere Volkswirtschaft. Meine Damen und Herren, obwohl es manche gar nicht so gerne wissen wollen, aber ein funktionierendes Bankensystem, die Finanzindustrie, ist das Nervensystem einer Volkswirtschaft. Ohne Banken, ohne Finanzindustrie kann es keine erfolgreiche Volkswirtschaft geben. Eine Finanzindustrie schafft die Grundlage für Wachstum und für die Zukunftsfähigkeit eines Landes. Ihr Bankenverband, der Bankenverband leistet dazu einen wichtigen Beitrag. Und deswegen gratuliere ich Ihnen noch einmal sehr herzlich zu Ihrem Jubiläum und ich wünsche Ihnen weiter in den nächsten Jahren und Jahrzehnten Glück und Erfolg bei dem, was Sie für unser Land tun. Was Sie getan haben in den letzten 75 Jahren – und ein solcher Geburtstag lädt ja ein, einen kurzen Rückblick auch zu nehmen auf das Jahr Ihrer Gründung, auf das Jahr 1951. Deutschland befand sich unter Bundeskanzler Konrad Adenauer im Wiederaufbau – politisch, gesellschaftspolitisch, wirtschaftlich. Diese junge Bundesrepublik Deutschland gewinnt mit der ersten Revision des Besatzungsstatuts einen Teil ihrer außen- und sicherheitspolitischen, auch ihrer wirtschaftspolitischen Souveränität zurück. Als Gründungsmitglied der Montanunion und als neues Mitglied des Europarats findet diese Bundesrepublik Deutschland Schritt für Schritt und langsam zurück in die zivilisierte Staatengemeinschaft. Das war damals alles andere als selbstverständlich. Und Deutschland erlebt ein Wirtschaftswunder, wie es später einmal heißen sollte, obwohl es ganz gewiss kein Wunder war. Denn es sind die Rahmenbedingungen gewesen, die damals mit dem klaren Bekenntnis zur sozialen Marktwirtschaft, zur offenen Volkswirtschaft, zu offenen Märkten dies erst überhaupt ermöglicht hat. Und meine Damen und Herren, in diesem Gründungsjahr Ihres Verbandes lag die Wachstumsrate der deutschen Volkswirtschaft bei 8,3 %. Nun war das eben alles eben kein Wunder. Es war das Ergebnis erheblicher Einsatzbereitschaft in der Bevölkerung. Es war das Ergebnis kluger Politik und richtiger ordnungspolitischer Weichenstellungen auch im Bankenwesen. Übrigens, aus der Rückschau verklärt sich manches. Es war nicht unumstritten. Im Gegenteil: Es musste hart errungen werden, schon vor der Gründung der Bundesrepublik Deutschland, etwa mit dem Eintritt in die freikonvertierbare Währung. Das, was Ludwig Erhard ermöglichte mit der sogenannten Währungsreform, war hart umstritten. Die deutschen Gewerkschaften haben versucht, dagegen einen Generalstreik auszurufen. Ich sage das alles deshalb, meine Damen und Herren, weil wir auch heute in solchen Zeiten leben – hart umstrittener politischer Entscheidungen. Denn so wie Deutschland nach dem Zweiten Weltkrieg auch als Finanzstandort neu aufgebaut werden musste, mit stabiler Währung und einem verlässlichen Bankensystem, so mussten wir auch in anderen Branchen, insbesondere in unserer Industrie, zurückkommen auf den Weg von Wachstum und neuer Beschäftigung. Und zu dieser Wahrheit gehört auch dazu, wie in anderen Branchen auch: Viele Bankhäuser haben personelle Kontinuitäten aus der NS-Zeit aufweisen müssen. Viele Banken waren zuvor in Kriegswirtschaft und wirtschaftliche Ausbeutung verstrickt. Diese Geschichte wurde spät aufgearbeitet, aber sie wurde aufgearbeitet. Meine Damen und Herren, der Bankenverband, der Bundesverband deutscher Banken gründete sich also in den Jahren des umfassenden Umbruchs. Sein erster Vorsitzender wurde der Bankier und Abgeordnete des Deutschen Bundestags, Robert Pferdmenges – ein enger Freund und Berater unseres ersten Bundeskanzlers und, so sagen es jedenfalls manche Quellen, sein einziger Duzfreund. Im Vorwort des ersten Geschäftsberichts des Verbandes berichtet Pferdmenges von der überraschenden Fülle an Aufgaben, die sich dem Verband in kürzester Zeit stellte. Ja, meine Damen und Herren, es war viel zu tun im Wiederaufbau. Die privaten Banken hatten sich zusammengeschlossen, gemeinsame Interessen zu vertreten, Standards zu setzen und die Wirtschaft verlässlich zu finanzieren für Wachstum und Wohlstand. Und dieser Auftrag wird nun von Ihnen seit 75 Jahren erfolgreich erfüllt. Am heutigen Tag gilt mein Dank also nicht nur Christian Sewing und Heiner Hackenhoff, sondern allen, die sich im Bankenverband über Jahre und Jahrzehnte engagiert haben und denjenigen, die sich heute in diesem Verband als verantwortungsvolle Teile unserer Finanzwirtschaft engagieren und einsetzen. Herzlichen Dank für Ihre Arbeit. Wir erleben heute erneut große Umbrüche. Christian Sewing hat darauf angehört... angesprochen. Während wir im Wiederaufbau der 50er Jahre die Herausforderungen unmittelbarerer und existenzieller erlebt haben, sind sie heute weniger greifbar. Und gerade deshalb ist das, was wir tun und tun müssen, anspruchsvoller. Ich beklage mich nicht darüber. Aber meine Damen und Herren, im Kern ist es doch dasselbe. Wir müssen wieder tragfähige Grundlagen für den Erfolg unseres Landes in den kommenden Jahrzehnten legen und wieder Wachstum und weiterhin Wohlstand ermöglichen. Und jetzt: Wir leben in Zeiten von geopolitischen und tiefen geoökonomischen Konflikten und Unsicherheiten, von anhaltenden Krisenlagen, von Krieg in Europa, von neuem Protektionismus, der sich gegen Freihandel und offene Märkte richtet. Und all das wirkt sich aus auf den wirtschaftlichen Erfolg oder eben Misserfolg unseres Landes. Die Lage bleibt angespannt, gerade auch im Mittleren und Nahen Osten. Die Auswirkungen dieses Krieges auf Wachstum und wirtschaftliche Entwicklung in Deutschland hängen maßgeblich von seiner Dauer und seiner Intensität ab. Und die Ereignisse der letzten Stunden lassen uns nicht ohne Hoffnung bleiben, dass es vielleicht doch zu einem baldigen Ende kommen könnte. Und dennoch: Wie immer, auch in einer sich verändernden Welt, liegen nicht nur Risiken, sondern auch Chancen. Aber wir werden diese Chancen nur nutzen, wenn wir unsere Wettbewerbsfähigkeit eben entschlossen stärken. Und genau das wollen wir tun. Es geht vor allem die preisliche Wettbewerbsfähigkeit unserer Unternehmen am Standort Deutschland. Und diese preisliche Wettbewerbsfähigkeit, sie ist seit Jahren nicht mehr gut genug. Wir haben es uns angewöhnt, mit kleinen Trippelschritten zu versuchen, einer Entwicklung hinterherzukommen, die wir dann aber dennoch nicht eingeholt haben. Deswegen müssen wir, deswegen setzen wir als Bundesregierung alles daran, die strukturellen Verwerfungen, die strukturellen Defizite unseres Landes zu beheben. Wir wollen die Wettbewerbsfähigkeit verbessern. Wir wollen niedrigere Energiekosten. Wir haben die ersten Schritte getan. Wir wollen niedrigere Unternehmenssteuern. Wir haben die ersten Schritte getan. Wir wollen weniger Bürokratie. Wir haben die ersten Schritte getan. Wir wollen gezielte Investitionen in Innovation und Infrastruktur. Und wir wollen Reformen der sozialen Sicherungssysteme. Meine Damen und Herren, nur dann, wenn wir sie reformieren, bleiben sie bezahlbar und zukunftsfest. Ich sage offen, und kurz vor dem ersten Jahresjubiläum unserer Regierungstätigkeit: Wir haben einiges erreicht, aber wir sind weit davon entfernt, dass es genug ist. Wir haben vor einer Woche in der Koalition erste kleinere weitere Schritte besprochen. Wir müssen und werden den Anstieg der Sozialversicherungsbeiträge stoppen. Wir müssen und werden eine strukturelle Reform der gesetzlichen Krankenversicherung auf den Weg bringen. Wir haben uns am letzten Wochenende darauf verständigt. Wir wollen – und mehr ist im Augenblick gar nicht erreichbar – wenigstens die Beitragssätze dauerhaft stabil halten. Nur damit Sie ein Gefühl für die Dimension bekommen, über die wir sprechen: 1 % Sozialversicherungsbeitrag in Deutschland sind 20 Milliarden Euro Sozialversicherungsbeiträge der Arbeitnehmerinnen, Arbeitnehmer und der Unternehmen. In Wahrheit sind es die Beiträge allein der Arbeitnehmerinnen und Arbeitnehmer. Denn das, was wir tun mit dem aufgeteilten Beitragssatz für Unternehmen und Beschäftigte, ist in Wahrheit nur eine Verrechnungsposition. Jeder Arbeitnehmer und jede Arbeitnehmerin muss mit seiner, ihrer Arbeitskraft diesen vollen Sozialversicherungsbeitrag erwirtschaften, sonst ist der Arbeitsplatz nicht wettbewerbsfähig. Wir sprechen zwei Prozentpunkte, die wir bei der gesetzlichen Krankenversicherung einsparen wollen, die ansonsten im nächsten Jahr drohen durch eine Reform. Mit anderen Worten: Diese Reform muss 40 Milliarden Euro einsparen auf der Ausgabenseite unserer gesetzlichen Krankenversicherung. Wir wollen im Sommer den Empfehlungen der Rentenreformkommission folgen, die eine umfassende Reform unserer Alterssicherungssysteme vorschlagen wird. Meine Damen und Herren, die gesetzliche Rentenversicherung allein wird allenfalls noch die Basisabsicherung sein für das Alter. Sie wird nicht mehr ausreichen auf Dauer, den Lebensstandard zu sichern. Es müssen kapitalgedeckte Elemente einer betrieblichen und privaten Altersvorsorge hinzutreten, und zwar in weit größerem Umfang, als wir sie gegenwärtig weitgehend auf der Basis von Freiwilligkeit haben. Hier kommen Sie ins Spiel, die Banken. Wir brauchen dabei Ihre Unterstützung, nicht nur materiell, sondern auch ideell und gesellschaftspolitisch. Zu zeigen, dass kapitalgedeckte Altersvorsorgesysteme auch unabhängiger werden von Demografie und Arbeitsmarkt, wird ein wichtiger Beitrag sein, den wir alle, den auch Sie leisten können und müssen zur Reform dieses Altersvorsorgesystems. Ich will es auch gerne sagen: Dass wir uns darauf im Koalitionsvertrag verständigen konnten, ist für uns Christdemokraten ein kleiner Schritt. Für die Sozialdemokraten war es ein großer. Anzuerkennen, dass wir jetzt ein Gesamtversorgungsniveau brauchen, das weit über die gesetzliche Rentenversicherung hinaus Bestandteile enthält, die dauerhaft und zuverlässig eingerichtet werden, ist für die Sozialdemokraten ein großer Schritt. Aber ich will das hinzufügen: Wir müssen ihn jetzt auch gemeinsam gehen. Und wir dürfen keinen Zweifel daran lassen, dass wir es wollen und dass wir es noch in diesem Jahr so auf den Weg bringen, dass es zum 1.1.2027 in Kraft tritt. Und meine Damen und Herren, das, was wir da vorhaben, betrifft wiederum in einem zweiten Aspekt unmittelbar Ihre Industrie, Ihre Unternehmen. Wir verbessern damit ja nicht nur die private und betriebliche Altersvorsorge. Wir verbessern damit auch durchgreifend den deutschen Kapitalmarkt. Und diejenigen, die mich kennen, wissen, dass ich das seit Jahren beklage, dass Sie in Deutschland eben keinen wirklich breiten und tiefen Kapitalmarkt haben. Keinen Kapitalmarkt, der herangezogen wird zur Altersvorsorge, aber eben auch keinen Kapitalmarkt, der im Umfang der notwendig wäre zur Verfügung steht für die Finanzierung der Unternehmen. Und hier sind Sie erneut auch ein Mitspieler, eine Institution, die beitragen und helfen muss. Ich möchte, dass wir in Deutschland Schritt für Schritt die Unternehmensfinanzierung auch sehr viel stärker kapitalmarktorientiert ausbauen. Und das heißt nicht, dass Sie als Banken daran keinen Anteil mehr haben in der Finanzierung – nein, ganz und gar nicht. Sie wechseln nur sozusagen die Seite der Bilanz. Und Sie helfen mit, dass die Eigenkapitalausstattung unserer Unternehmen besser wird und die Abhängigkeit von Fremdkapital geringer. Das ist die Aufgabe, der wir uns in dieser Wahlperiode stellen müssen. Wenn wir das schaffen, haben wir ein großes Potenzial in Deutschland. Es fehlt nicht an Geld. Es fehlt an mobilisierten Mitteln für den Kapitalmarkt und spiegelbildlich damit für die Altersvorsorge in unserem Lande, meine Damen und Herren. Und dass dafür eine große Bereitschaft in Deutschland besteht, dies auch mit Investitionen und Innovationen zu verbinden, das konnte ich gestern und heute bis in den frühen Nachmittag hinein in Hannover auf der diesjährigen Industriemesse 2026 erleben. Ich will an dieser Stelle ein Wort des Dankes an Christian Sewing sagen. ist einer der Mitinitiatoren der Initiative "Made for Germany". Wir haben heute Nachmittag, heute Mittag ein Treffen dort gehabt. Mittlerweile weit über 100 Unternehmen, die sich verpflichtet haben, über 800 Milliarden Euro in den nächsten fünf Jahren in den deutschen Standort zu investieren. Meine Damen und Herren, wenn Sie mit diesen Unternehmen sprechen, auch mit den zum Teil jungen Startup-Unternehmen sprechen, die dabei sind, dann bekommen Sie ein Gefühl dafür, welches Potenzial, welche Dynamik, welcher Wille zur Investition und zur Innovation in unserem Lande vorhanden ist. Ich bin fest entschlossen, dieses Kapital und diese Sympathie und dieses Engagement zu nutzen für unser Land. Wir müssen es gemeinsam tun, damit wir aus dieser strukturellen Schwäche unserer Volkswirtschaft gemeinsam herauskommen. Ich bin Christian Sewing dankbar, dass die Europäische Union angesprochen hat. Wir sind mit Abstand das bevölkerungsreichste Land dieser Europäischen Union mit über 82 Millionen Einwohnern von 450 in der EU. Wenn Deutschland nicht einen überproportionalen Beitrag zum Gelingen des europäischen Projekts leistet, dann wird dieses europäische Projekt scheitern. Und umgekehrt: Wenn Europa sich mit Binnenmarkt und Wettbewerbsfähigkeit, mit dem, was Enrico Letta und Mario Draghi uns bei einer Offside am 12. Februar im ehemaligen Schloss der Gründung des Deutschen Ordens in der Nähe von Lüttich gesagt haben... Wenn Europa diesen Weg nicht geht, dann wird auch Deutschland scheitern. Kein Land in dieser Europäischen Union ist vom Gelingen des Binnenmarkts und der Wettbewerbsfähigkeit Europas so abhängig wie wir. Und gleichzeitig kein anderes Land kann zum Gelingen dieses großen Projekts in der zweiten Phase einen so großen Beitrag leisten wie wir, meine Damen und Herren. Und deswegen mag mich der eine oder andere nicht hier im Saal, aber außerhalb, als "Außenkanzler" apostrophieren. Ich werde an diesem Engagement festhalten, mich in Europa dafür einzusetzen, dass wir gemeinsam in Europa erfolgreich sind – nicht nur ökonomisch, nicht nur wirtschaftspolitisch, sondern dass wir auch unsere sicherheitspolitischen, unsere verteidigungspolitischen Interessen gemeinsam wahrnehmen in dieser schweren Zeit, in der wir sind. Ohne Europa wird uns das auch nicht gelingen, meine Damen und Herren. – Wir haben großartige Unternehmen, wir haben großartige Mitarbeiterinnen und Mitarbeiter, wir haben eine im Wesentlichen jedenfalls gut ausgebildete Mitarbeiterschaft in den Unternehmen. Wir haben junge, innovative Unternehmen, die in Europa wachsen wollen, die Kapital aufnehmen und an die Börse gehen wollen. Ich sage diesen Unternehmen: Wenn es eben möglich ist, tut es in Europa und nicht unbedingt in den USA. Ich möchte, dass der Kapitalmarkt in Europa so stark wird, so liquide wird, dass die Kapitalsammelstellen, die wir haben, so stark werden, dass sie auch selbst einen Beitrag dazu leisten können, dass der europäische Kapitalmarkt stärker wird. Ich möchte, dass wir den Verbriefungsmarkt so schnell wie möglich wiederbeleben, dass wir eine leistungsfähige europäische Finanzmarktinfrastruktur bekommen. Genau dafür setzt sich die Bundesregierung ein. Genau dafür setze ich mich auch persönlich mit allem Gewicht, das ich auf die Waage bringen kann, auf europäischer Ebene ein. Und von einem vertieften Kapitalmarkt, meine Damen und Herren, profitieren Sie alle. Wir zählen auf Sie auf allen Ebenen – mit hochvolumigen Finanzierungen, mit Börsengängen können oft nur die größten Banken es wirklich leisten und stemmen. Ja, wir brauchen große Banken in Europa. Aber lassen Sie mich aus aktuellem Anlass eines auch sehr klar sagen: Dies bedeutet nicht, dass jede Form und jede Art der Übernahme in Deutschland willkommen ist. Feindliches, aggressives Vorgehen lehnen wir entschieden ab. Wir müssen auf das schauen, was wir im europäischen und im deutschen Markt gemeinsam brauchen. Und unser Mittelstand, unser Handwerk sind auf lokal verankerte Finanzierung angewiesen. In vielen Feldern macht hier auch der Beitrag durch kleine und mittlere Banken vor Ort den Unterschied. Draghi und Letta machen übrigens einen zweiten weiteren wichtigen Punkt: Wir haben zu viel Regulierung und Bürokratie in Europa. Auch das hemmt Innovation und Wachstum. Ich setze mich daher seit meinem Amtsantritt dafür ein, auf europäischer Ebene auch einen Rückbau von Bürokratie zu ermöglichen. Unsere Unternehmen dürfen nicht länger von diesen unnötigen Wachstumshindernissen stehen – und sie stehen vor solchen Hürden. Denn das ist Bürokratie. Das schadet unserer Wettbewerbsfähigkeit. Ich erwarte deshalb von der Europäischen Kommission, und ich sage es immer wieder, dass sie das gesamte europäische Recht überprüft mit zwei Zielen, nämlich bestehende Gesetze zu vereinfachen und Überregulierungen ersatzlos zu streichen. Die Kommission hat bereits einige Vereinfachungspakete, sogenannte Omnibus-Pakete, auf den Weg gebracht und weitere sind geplant. Das ist der richtige Ansatz, auch für die Finanzmarktregulierung. Übrigens, nach der Finanzkrise wurden wichtige und richtige Entscheidungen getroffen, die die Widerstandsfähigkeit im Finanzsektor deutlich verbessert haben. Das Eigenkapital ist höher, die Risikopuffer sind größer – das bleibt richtig und wichtig. Aber es ist nun an der Zeit, diese Regulierung auch erneut auf den Prüfstand zu stellen, meine Damen und Herren. Nicht alles, was unmittelbar nach der Finanzkrise richtig war, bleibt heute richtig – auch angesichts des Wettbewerbs, den wir zum Beispiel mit den amerikanischen Banken auf dem europäischen Kontinent austragen müssen. Ich will es deshalb begrüßen, begrüße es daher sehr ausdrücklich, dass die Europäische Kommission an einem Bericht zur Wettbewerbsfähigkeit der europäischen Banken arbeitet. Auf den Bericht muss ein Vereinfachungspaket folgen. Dafür setze ich mich ein, dafür setzt sich die Bundesregierung ein gemeinsam mit anderen Mitgliedstaaten. Die Finanzstabilität muss natürlich gesichert, aber gleichzeitig muss die Bürokratie deutlich zurückgebaut werden – Berichtspflichten reduziert werden. Proportionalität, meine Damen und Herren, ist das richtige Stichwort. Auch das senkt Kosten für die Banken in Deutschland. Und schließlich: Wir müssen in Deutschland die Umsetzung von Regulierung im Blick behalten und zielgerichtete, verhältnismäßige und vor allem bürokratiearme Umsetzungen ermöglichen. "Gold-plating" nennt man so etwas in Deutschland, wenn es darum geht, Richtlinien der Europäischen Union nicht eins zu eins, sondern eins zu 1,5 oder gar noch weitergehend umzusetzen. Was für ganz Europa genug ist, das kann und muss auch für Deutschland ausreichend sein, meine Damen und Herren. In diesem Sinne: 75 Jahre Bankenverband, Bundesverband deutscher Banken – das ist nicht nur ein dankbarer Blick zurück, das ist auch ein Auftrag für die Zukunft. Denn wenn wir eines aus Ihrer und unserer Geschichte seit 1951 lernen können, dann ist es dies: Fortschritte werden gerade in Zeiten großer Herausforderungen möglich. Die Welt sortiert sich im Augenblick neu. Ja, unsere Politik, unsere Weichenstellungen gerade jetzt entscheiden deshalb, wie stark unser Land morgen sein wird. Und ob aus Unsicherheit neue Stärke entsteht, ob aus Umbrüchen neue Chancen werden – das, und jetzt hat Christian Sewing erneut recht, liegt an uns. Es liegt in unserer Hand. Dass das gelingt, daran arbeiten wir, daran arbeitet die Bundesregierung, daran arbeiten Sie. Und es kommt wieder auf Sie an, auf die Finanzwirtschaft, wie schon in den Anfangsjahren der Bundesrepublik Deutschland. Wenn wir, wenn alle in diesem Land klug zusammenwirken, wenn alle bereit sind, ihren Teil beizutragen und ihren Teil auch zu tragen, dann können wir die Zukunft unseres Landes erfolgreich gestalten, und zwar so, dass wirtschaftlicher Erfolg und ein gutes gesellschaftliches Miteinander ebenso möglich sind wie persönliche Verwirklichungschancen eines jeden Einzelnen. Das geht Hand in Hand. Das leben Sie vor. Das sind realistische Zielvorstellungen. Dafür, meine Damen und Herren, braucht es kein Wunder. Dafür braucht es jetzt Leistung zuversichtlicher und verantwortungsvoller Menschen, die gemeinsam die großartigen Potenziale verwirklichen, die unser Land auch unverändert heute hat. Noch einmal in diesem Sinne, lieber Christian Sewing, herzlichen Glückwunsch zum 75. Geburtstag, auf die nächsten erfolgreichen Jahrfünfte und Jahrzehnte. Reformen der sozialen Sicherungssysteme, eine Einkommensteuerreform zum nächsten Jahr und dann Innovation und Investition zu ermöglichen. Wir alle müssen uns anstrengen, wir müssen jetzt die Ärmel aufkrempeln und wir müssen Schritt für Schritt dieses Land wieder fit machen. Ich komme aus Hannover zurück mit wirklich großem Optimismus. Wir haben Brasilien als Partnerland dort gehabt, wir hatten deutsch-brasilianische Wirtschaftsgespräche, die 33., wir hatten die dritten deutsch-brasilianischen Regierungskonsultationen. Staatspräsident Lula war da mit der Hälfte seiner... mehr als der Hälfte seiner Regierung. Wir haben auch mal abgeglichen, was die Südamerikaner besser können als wir – übrigens Biofuels, synthetische Kraftstoffe. Da kommt keiner auf die Schnapsidee, den Verbrenner zu verbieten. Die betreiben den Verbrenner mit synthetischen, CO2-neutralen Kraftstoffen. So, so möchte ich, dass wir denken, so dass wir rauskommen aus diesen Blockaden, die wir hier haben. Da können wir von Brasilien einiges lernen, wir können in der Kooperation einiges lernen und wir können glaube ich auch zeigen, welches Potenzial an Innovation in Deutschland da ist und das muss nicht nur in Hannover sein, das sollte im ganzen Land stattfinden. – Aber da fragt sich vielleicht der eine oder andere, warum ist das eigentlich bis dato noch nicht passiert? Das sind ja Dinge, die man schon hätte vorantreiben können, trotz des Krieges beispielsweise im Iran. – Wir müssen es wegen des Krieges weiter vorantreiben. Und ich werde heute Abend auch noch einmal mit dem Koalitionspartner sehr ernsthaft reden. Ich habe das eben in meiner Rede gesagt und ich will es hier noch einmal wiederholen: Mir reicht das nicht, was wir bisher geschafft haben. Wir laufen immer noch hinter der Kurve. Wir sind nicht vor der Kurve. Sondern wir müssen jetzt sehen, dass wir sehr schnell diese Reformen auch durchsetzen. Ich erwarte von den Sozialdemokraten, dass sie auch manche Blockade auflösen, die wir in den letzten Wochen und Monaten leider immer wieder gehabt haben. Wir brauchen in der Kraftwerksstrategie jetzt ein grünes Licht, wir brauchen auch in der Korrektur unserer Ausgaben, zum Beispiel für Wind und Sonne, eine wirkliche schnelle Entscheidung des Bundeskabinetts. Wir wollen weiter ausbauen, Wind und Sonne – ja, wir wollen es. Aber es macht doch überhaupt keinen Sinn, für Energie, die erzeugt wird und nicht gebraucht wird, trotzdem hohe Subventionen aus dem Bundeshaushalt zu bezahlen. Das können wir uns nicht länger leisten und deswegen stehen wir jetzt vor einigen wichtigen Entscheidungen. – Innenpolitisch wie außenpolitisch... genau, innenpolitisch wie außenpolitisch vor großen Herausforderungen. Nehmen wir die Straße von Hormus, gerade auch mit Blick der Auswirkungen der Weltwirtschaft. Da überschlagen sich ja die Meldungen. Einmal ist sie blockiert von der einen Seite, dann von beiden Seiten, dann wieder geöffnet, dann wieder geschlossen. Dann gibt es Beschuss. Und wo sage ich das hier? Beim Bankenverband. Wir haben 2008 die Finanzkrise erlebt, das war ein Schock über Nacht, der uns anschließend zwei Jahre intensiv beschäftigt hat. Also jetzt: Jede Krise ist natürlich diejenige, die man persönlich am meisten empfindet. Aber es ist doch nicht so, als ob wir nicht Erfahrungen hätten mit Krisen. Corona war eine Krise, die uns drei Jahre beschäftigt hat. Die Rückstände, die in der Zeit entstanden sind, haben wir nicht wieder aufgeholt. Wir haben uns ausgeruht, wir sind ein bisschen zu bequem geworden. Und wenn man heute nach China schaut, selbst wenn man nach Amerika schaut – die Dynamik, die in diesen Volkswirtschaften nach wie vor oder jetzt vor allen Dingen in China vorhanden ist, die fehlt uns. Und ich möchte ganz einfach, dass wir etwas schneller werden, dass wir besser werden, dass wir auch die Chancen dieser Krise jetzt nutzen. Was heißt das denn, die Welt ordnet sich gerade neu? Das ist doch... Christian Sewing hat es eben mit dieser Zahl gesagt, ich habe sie wiederholt: 450 Millionen Einwohner. Ja, bei den Sozialversicherungsbeiträgen – wir müssen jetzt solche Reformen machen. Die Wahrheit. Und ich versuche, die NATO, die wir haben und die uns 75 Jahre geschützt hat, auch für die nächsten Jahre zu erhalten. So. Und wir arbeiten daran, dass Europa auch besser wird in der Verteidigung. Und das persönliche Verhältnis – ja, es ist wie es ist.
