Aber ich habe ihn nicht gewählt, ich bin auch nicht gefragt worden, aber ist der Präsident der Vereinigten Staaten von Amerika und der deutsche Bundeskanzler muss mit jedem Präsidenten der Vereinigten Staaten von Amerika versuchen, vernünftig auszukommen. Und das tue ich. – And now I'll switch to English because we are joined by one of the most influential voices in global finance, by the head of the European Central Bank. Please welcome Christine Lagarde. Welcome. The stage is yours. – Damen und Herren, guten Tag. Good afternoon. It's lovely to be here. And it's particularly lovely because, for once, the preceding speaker is even taller than me. It is so often the case that I have to go... bend. Now, no. Fantastic. It was a great pleasure to listen to the Chancellor and it is an even greater pleasure and a great honor to be the second speaker tonight. And Christian, I would like to thank you as President of the association for having invited me on this occasion. So what I understand is that when this association was founded, it was a new beginning for Germany. Europe had come out of that period of time devastated by a tragic period in its modern history. But it was about to enter into the golden age of peace and economic growth as described very ably by the Chancellor. Fast forward to today and clearly we are in a far more uncertain situation, very uncertain about the direction Europe will take and probably one of the most difficult at any point in time in the last 75 years. And much of that uncertainty is not from within. It's from outside our borders. My suspicion is that when historians look back at this period, what will stand out is the sheer relentlessness of it all. A once-in-a-generation pandemic, followed by a land war on our continent at our doorstep, followed by the worst energy crisis in 50 years – not this one, remember '22 – followed by the most sweeping tariff increase since the 30s. And now, a military conflict that has shut down the world's most important energy shock point, the Strait of Hormuz. I have to tell you that in our grimmest scenarios back in 2020, when we had in mind that this could happen, the worst that could happen at the time in our scenario was a third of the traffic being halted. A third. Now, each of these shocks that I have just mentioned has torn away something that Europe had come to take for granted: a secure supply of cheap energy; a predictable trading relationship with the United States; the foundation of our post-war military security. I'm going to ask for your indulgence at this point in time because I will try to quote Hegel in German. So bear with me. "Die hohle de... der Minerva beginnt erst mit der einbrechenden Dämmerung ihren Flug." For those who did not understand my German, you are forgiven. And the English goes as follows: "The owl of Minerva begins its flight only as dusk falls." Why would she say that? Probably because I like owls. You know, I'm not a hawk, I'm not a dove, I'm an owl. But I'm saying that because it means understanding comes after the experimentation, not before it. And the truth is that these painful years have taught us a great deal already. And some of those lessons are already being acted upon. The transformation of defense policy in Germany over the past year would have been unthinkable, unthinkable without the shocks that preceded it. But there are also lessons on how we navigate the economic consequences of large shocks, and those lessons are directly relevant to the situation that we are facing today. So building on this, I'm going to focus on three particular areas. One, I'm going to try to figure out at this time, at 10 to 7 p.m. today, where the economy stands. Two, what monetary policy needs to see to calibrate its reaction. And three, how fiscal policies can help keep the inflation shock as small as possible. The economic picture remains deeply uncertain, you've heard that many times already. The stop-start-stop nature of the conflict – war, ceasefire, peace talks, they collapse, a naval blockade, it's lifting, it's reinstatement – makes it exceptionally hard to gauge the duration and depth of the consequences. Hence the reason I'm saying at 7 p.m. today, who knows what comes next. But it is clear that there is no easy path back to where we were before the conflict erupted. It will be different. In March, we published our baseline projection for the economy alongside two alternative scenarios: an adverse scenario and a worse-than-adverse, a severe scenario. Each driven by higher energy prices. And all three, the baseline, the severe and the adverse, all three of them saw higher inflation and lower growth than what we expected back in December. With the damage escalating with the severity and the duration of the shock. Now the critical question that we ask ourselves – and this will be obviously the case in future monetary policy meetings that we have – is which path are we on? Are we baseline? Are we severe? Are we adverse? The supply disruption is enormous. Even if you account for the pipeline redirection by some of the Gulf countries. And if you account for the release of some of the strategic reserves, the net loss of oil is estimated at around 13 million barrels per day. That is 13 % of global consumption. And that is before the U.S. blockade. So at a time when the Iranian shipment could actually travel all the way to India or China. But you observe as much as I do how markets react. And so far, we have not seen energy prices rise far enough to push us squarely into the adverse scenario. While both oil spot and future prices are above what we assumed in our baseline, markets appear to be betting that the disruption will be short-lived. European natural gas prices are below our baseline, reflecting among other things, gas-to-coal switch in Asia and relatively mild weather in China so far. If the conflict resolves quickly, the direct energy price shock could prove to be at the smaller end of expectations. And the economic impact could be contained. But the outlook remains fragile and worse paths are still possible. Every day the conflict continues, the wider the gap between energy supply and demand, the longer the normalization. And the longer the disruption, the further the spread of the effects, not only through higher energy costs but through the loss of critical input. Let me mention three of them. Around a third of helium... now we've all become chemical experts. I had never heard about helium before. And I'm sure many of you know what helium was about a month ago, but I'm not sure. So, a third of helium produced globally comes from the Gulf and its loss would be felt severely in the semiconductor fabrication and in high-tech manufacturing. Example number one. Example number two. Close to a fifth of global methanol production is also at risk, with severe consequences for the chemicals and plastic industries. And third, a third of the fertilizers is shipped through the Strait of Hormuz. Now, that is also at risk. And it matters particularly in the Southern hemisphere, where the planting and therefore the fertilizers is badly needed now. I'm not saying that just out of interest for agriculture. Don't forget I was Minister of Agriculture in France for a month and a half. But because it matters to me as a central banker now. Because if the price of food increases significantly, it is not going to just be the price of food, it is going to be inflation expectations. Because we know that people are particularly attentive to two things: the price of food, processed and unprocessed, and the price of gas at the petrol station. So here are three indirect consequences of what's happening in the energy world. So if the disruption persists long enough, the adjustment shifts from prices, which we are putting up with now, to possibly rationing with very different economic consequences. Higher prices are primarily inflationary. Shortages hit output directly and are worse for growth. Overall there have been so far limited signs of supply chain disruption, both globally and in the euro area. But local tensions are visible. Jet fuel prices have roughly doubled since the outbreak of the conflict and rationing has been imposed at some individual airports in Europe since early April. And I'm not talking about what's happening in the rest of the world. I'm just back from the IMF and the World Bank spring meetings, like many of you, and you might have bumped into colleagues from Asia, in particular some of the low-income countries or emerging market economies that are taking a much more severe hit than us and are moving into rationing. – So setting monetary policy in this environment is challenging, I can assure you. We know we're facing a major shock. And if you listen to Fatih Birol, the head of the International Energy Agency, he thinks that it is the largest oil supply disruptions in history. But past experience has taught us that the size of the shock alone does not determine its economic consequences. There are two other factors which are critically important to really assess the size of the shock and to determine what is the best policy response. The first is the duration of the disruption. As the economic picture has just described makes clear, the longer the conflict lasts, the worse the outlook becomes and not in a linear way. Duration is as a result key for judging which scenario we are facing. In '22, it was clear: we were going to move away from Russian gas for good. Took a little while, but it was not... there was no way back. We had to build new LNG infrastructure. Germany set standards in that respect. We had to find new suppliers and we had to compete in the global market for imports. Today, the range of possible outcomes is far wider. On the 31st of March, when the conflict appeared to be escalating, oil prices would have placed us squarely in the adverse scenario. Ten days later, after the ceasefire was announced, we were in between the adverse and the baseline. We're watching what comes next. The second factor, in addition to the duration, is the pass-through of energy prices to broader inflation. What other people would call the second-round effect. The same energy shock can play out very differently depending on the economic environment in which it lands. Remember 2022: we were coming out of COVID, there was strong demand, global supply chains had bottlenecks right, left and center, and acute labor shortages created the conditions for a broad pass-through. Compare that not with today, but when energy prices spiked in 2008 and 2011. The economies were weaker. Remember Global Financial Crisis, the European sovereign debt crisis. And it meant that higher prices stayed largely in the energy compartment. Well, what about today? Today we see two forces pulling in opposite directions and they're both likely to influence the inflation response this time. First force: households and firms, corporates, your clients, have just lived through a large inflation shock and may be more sensitive to rising costs. The muscle memory is fresh. Incoming surveys suggest that the selling price expectations of firms have increased and that households are already paying much more attention to prices. The other force: higher energy prices and weaker consumer sentiment will weigh on demand, particularly given that growth while recovering was moderate before the conflict began. That could limit the extent to which prices and wages increase. The negotiations your clients will be having, both in terms of salary increases and in terms of invoicing to customers, will be critically important and extremely informative to us. Because that could limit the extent of price and wage increase. In the two most recent comparable episodes, the Gulf War of 1990-1991 and the Russia invasion of Ukraine, oil supply shocks reduced euro area GDP by around 0.4 % on average in the first year. And the relative importance of these two forces that I have just identified – the memory muscle on the one hand, the dampened possibly demand on the other hand – that will only become clear as we see actual data on firms' pricing behavior and wage negotiations. This double uncertainty about the duration of the shock and the breath of pass-through argues for gathering more information before drawing firm conclusions for our monetary policy. – But there is another factor that will shape the inflation outcome: the design of fiscal policy. I'm sorry that the Chancellor really had to go because I had a couple of messages for him. I've been in politics for six years of my life and I know how it feels. Governments around Europe are facing strong pressure. Pressure to cushion the blow for households when energy prices surge. But the 2022 experience shows that both approaches to providing support – whether they are price-based measures or income-based measures – involve difficult trade-offs. The price-based measures can bring down inflation but at the cost of blurring the signal that households and firms need to reduce energy consumption. Measures such as tax cuts and price caps reduced inflation by close to one percentage point in '22. This was valuable because energy prices are highly, as I said, visible to the public and weigh heavily on inflation expectations. But when such measures are broad and open-ended, the public has no incentive to cut back on energy use. And when they are eventually unwound – which Germany did as it had promised on the date it had promised, not all member states did that – but when they are unwound, they raise inflation mechanically. And during the last shock, the withdrawal of fiscal support contributed to extending the period of above-target inflation well into 2024 and 2025. That's for the price-based measure. Now the income-based measures can protect citizens but at the cost of stimulating the economy too much. Fiscal transfers to lower-income households are socially necessary and economically stabilizing. But when support is extended across the income distribution without any targeting, it sustains demand that firms can then use to pass on higher costs, forcing then monetary policy to tighten more than it would otherwise would. We have to learn from that. Particularly because today we have another constraint. In the years since the pandemic, an expectation has taken hold that governments of all colors and calibers will step in to shield households and firms from every large shock. But guess what? In the meantime, fiscal space has shrunk. Governments that try to cushion every shock for every household risk undermining fiscal sustainability at their peril. That's what I wanted to say to Chancellor. So it doesn't mean that government has no response, but it means that selectivity is not just desirable but necessary. The lesson of '22 is clear: support has to be temporary, so period of time at the expiry of which you waive it; targeted, focus on the most needed, the most in need; and preserves the price signal that can protect the move towards different energy mix. So to conclude: Europe faces a decisive moment now. And probably one of the most decisive in the last 70 years, 75 years since your association was founded. The pace of change is disorienting. For those of you who were last week in Washington, our heads were all over the place. Not much by way of a straight compass. Which reinforces my conviction that we poor central bankers, we have to stay focused on what we have to do, which is deliver on our mandate. 2 % medium-term target. We're not going to move away from that. The certainties of the past are vanishing. The allies are vanishing. Those who were friends could be foes. Those who were foes might be friends. One thing is certain: we can only be stronger together as Europeans. And I would advocate exactly what the Chancellor has advocated: one market, a strong capital market. Let's make sure that the massive savings that accumulate in many of your institutions are actually invested in this continent in Europe in order to make sure that we can be sovereign at home and autonomous as much as is possible. It might take a little while because defense requires massive investment in order to assure one of the attributes of an international currency of reserve. It will come. But we have to all do what is necessary. Be together and not bicker about stupid things when our survival is at stake. Thank you. – Thank you very much for your speech. And if it's okay with you, I'm going to jump right in and ask you a few more questions. Like you said, we keep seeing headlines like "Growth down, inflation up", obviously reflecting on what's going on in terms of the Iran war. At what point does inflation become high enough for long enough that it's no longer tenable to look through that transitory shock? – That's a good question to ask me a week before we start our quiet period. So you know, in times of such uncertainty, we cannot give forward guidance. I know that sometimes some investors and some market operators would like to have a forward guidance or thresholds – you know, "As of such level, we will do this" or "We will do that". I cannot tell you that. Because it's a time when you have to be on the lookout, check the data, verify them, cross-examine them, go really deep into the granular information that we can have access to in order to anticipate what is likely to affect the economy and how much it will. So the best we can do is: number one, observe the data very, very carefully – and you have no idea about the variety of data that we look at; operate with our scenarios so that we know pretty much, you know, in which category we are; and then calibrate our response on the basis of all that. So I cannot answer your question other than with that: data, with masses of it; scenario analysis, validation; and then calibration on the basis of our monetary policy stance determination that we have outlined for markets to understand as well as they can what is our thinking and how we will apply it. – I'll try another question. Which geoeconomic trends do you think will most fundamentally reshape the global economy in the coming years? – You know what? If we can manage to deliver on Letta-Draghi in short order, that could be a significant transformation that would actually reshape the current relationship that we see, which is very binary between the United States and China, with, you know, Russia playing this terrible role against Ukraine but not being a key player as opposed to the other two. But if we can actually get our act together, be one market, have a deep and liquid capital market, and leverage the talent and the innovation that we have, support the venture capitalists that can accompany those people – I think that could be transformational. I want to be optimistic. I think that what we are seeing at the moment will have a period of time and will come to an end. And we have work to do. That's what I think we should be concentrating on. Not look at the rest of the world and complain and, you know, feel miserable about it. But ask ourselves: what have we got? And as Christian said, we have 450 million consumers in Europe. We are aging, but so is China, so is the United States, especially if it pushes out all the migration that was coming into that country. And we have unbelievable strength in our educational system, in our democracy, in the respect of the key principles that we have abided by – including in particular the international rule of law. – So let's get more specific. If you had to prioritize one European project that would boost the continent's competitiveness? – Savings, Investment, and Union. – Alright. Done. That was easy. And very quickly before I let you go: You were the first woman to be made partner at a major multinational law firm. You were also the first woman to serve as economy minister in a G7 country, the first to lead the International Monetary Fund, the first at ECB. So, what "firsts" are still on your list? – Well tried. – Ich möchte mich selbstverständlich auch noch bei Ihnen bedanken, auch im Namen des BdB. Und danke an das ganze Team, das diesen Abend möglich gemacht hat. Jetzt geht es weiter im Saal 2, das ist einmal hier zu meiner Linken. Dort ist der Empfang. Watch CNBC's full interview with White House National Economic Council Director Kevin Hassett

CNBC Television(3.4M)·11:28·14.3K views·2026-04-20
Kevin HassettDirector, White House National Economic Council
TL;DRNEC Director Kevin Hassett signals high confidence in a swift resolution to the Iran conflict within weeks, predicting that U.S.
energy dominance and supply-side successes will drive long-term oil prices and inflation significantly lower. Iran Negotiations: Hassett...

TL;DR

NEC Director Kevin Hassett signals high confidence in a swift resolution to the Iran conflict within weeks, predicting that U.S. energy dominance and supply-side successes will drive long-term oil prices and inflation significantly lower.

Geopolitical Conflict and Energy Outlook

  • Iran Negotiations: Hassett maintains that peace talks are progressing positively, echoing President Trump’s "four to six week" resolution timeline. He attributes market resilience to the President's direct communication, which has reduced the "overhang" of uncertainty.
  • Oil Price Deflation: Despite current volatility, Hassett predicts a long-term downward trend for oil prices once the Strait of Hormuz risk premium is removed.
      • Global Supply: Saudi Arabia holds approximately 2 million barrels per day in excess capacity ready for export once the straits reopen.
      • U.S. Dominance: Every "empty vessel on Earth" is reportedly heading to the U.S. to load oil, highlighting the U.S. role as the world's largest producer.
  • UAE Support: Regarding reports of the UAE seeking currency swaps or bailouts due to economic injury from the closed strait, Hassett confirms the UAE is a "valuable ally" and suggests Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent is prepared to assist if necessary.

Economic Fundamentals and Inflation

  • Corporate Performance: The market’s "V-shaped" recovery is backed by two consecutive earnings seasons with nearly universal upside surprises, which Hassett labels as the beginning of a "Golden Age" for profit and wage data.
  • Inflation Progress: While headline inflation remains a concern, Hassett highlights that core inflation (2.6%) is at its lowest level since the start of the Biden administration.
      • Commodity Successes: Specific policy interventions have lowered costs for eggs, beef (via increased imports), and prescription drugs ("Trump RX").
      • Macro Strategy: The administration is fighting inflation through supply-side increases and government spending cuts, noting a $400 billion reduction in the deficit last year.
  • Household Impact: Hassett estimates that recent tax policies have provided $4,000 to $7,000 in relief for typical workers, retirees, and those earning tips or overtime.

Federal Reserve Leadership

  • Kevin Warsh Nomination: Hassett strongly defends Fed nominee Kevin Warsh, citing his experience at the NEC and as the youngest-ever Fed Governor during the financial crisis.
  • Confirmation Outlook: Despite political friction in the Senate (specifically regarding Senator Tom Tillis), Hassett expects Warsh to be confirmed once his "non-partisan" approach and "impeccable credentials" are displayed at hearings.
  • Transparency: Hassett suggests a Warsh-led Fed will provide better oversight and transparency regarding "cost overruns" and recent central bank management.
"The US economy is the engine of global growth, and so a strong US economy is super positive despite what’s going on in the Gulf... once this thing is done, think about the explosion in new energy production that could happen in the Middle East, driving prices way, way down over time." — Kevin Hassett
"2.6 is the lowest that core inflation has been since before Biden inflation started. And so we’ve made a huge amount of progress... we’re also of course addressing the macroeconomic issue of how you get inflation down, and that’s by being supply-side." — Kevin Hassett
"I think after the hearing, people will see what a strong candidate [Kevin Warsh] is... when he’s Fed chair, then everybody who wants to find out about all the cost overruns and everything else that’s been going on over the last couple of years will of course be able to do so because we’ve got a Fed chair there who people trust." — Kevin Hassett
Great day to have on Kevin Hassett. He’s the Director of the National Economic Council. Good morning, Mr. Director. Good to be here. Tell you where I want to start. Kevin, earlier we had a discussion about all the new highs last week, and the V-shaped bottom put in on this latest conflict. When it started, we noted that in previous conflicts like this, geopolitical conflicts, it’s usually very short-lived and usually a pretty good buying opportunity. But again, history never repeats, it always rhymes. For some reason, because of the strait, the issue of the strait, because of oil prices and what could happen with inflation and what that would do to the Fed, we thought, no, it’s not going to happen. But darn if it didn’t happen again to new highs in the face of all this uncertainty. What do you attribute that to? Right. Well, I think what’s been happening more than anything else is that President Trump has been communicating with the American people on what the current state of things is, and he’s been talking about how negotiations are moving forward well. He Truthed over the weekend that talks are resuming. Now, there are a lot of people chattering in Iran that aren’t necessarily in power who are trying to perhaps undermine their own colleagues. But the fact is that President Trump’s Truths—you go back and look one after another—have given a very clear guide to the journalists in the country and the American people about the progress that we’re making. At the beginning, the President said in four to six weeks we expect this could be resolved, and I think we’re getting pretty close to that being accurate. So if you remove the overhang or at least keep people updated, my point was that there must be some underlying fundamentals that you would point to, whether it's corporate profits or whether it's margins. I’m amazed at how well-behaved the bond market has been, with the 10-year staying right around in a range in spite of a lot of different concerns about debt and inflation and everything else. Well, as you know, we’ve had two earnings seasons in a row where basically everything surprised on the upside. That’s happening because the Golden Age that President Trump promised is starting to be very visible in the profit data, but also more importantly, in the wage data. If you’re a person who has a job where you’re making tips or you’re making overtime or you’re a retiree who thought that Social Security wasn’t what you expected and you need to make some more money, all of you got huge tax breaks in the $4,000 to $7,000 range because of the big, beautiful bill. And so I think that with a strong domestic economy, then the question is, is there something about the foreign relations that could upset that? And first, the US economy is the engine of global growth, and so a strong US economy is super positive despite what’s going on in the Gulf. But also, once this is resolved, think about how much energy production in the Gulf area has been depressed over time because of the huge risk premium associated with Iranian misbehavior. And so once this thing is done, think about the explosion in new energy production that could happen in the Middle East, driving prices way, way down over time, including in the US. And the final thing I can say is that if you look at the ship traffic data, it’s kind of like every empty vessel on Earth is headed towards the US to fill up with oil right now. And so the US is the biggest oil producer on Earth, and the capacity that we have to meet the demand of people who are stuck in that picture that you’re showing, I think is a positive surprise for markets. Kevin, notwithstanding whether you’re in support of the war or not and think that it’s worthwhile and that we can—you know, some near-term pain in terms of inflation and affordability—maybe it’s worth it in the long run for all the objectives that obviously we’ve outlined for why we went into Iran in the first place. But in terms of inflation, there’s a reason we give a headline number and there’s a reason that we give a core number, because food and energy are notoriously volatile on a monthly basis and they can give people the opportunity to say inflation tripled because it went from 0.3 to 0.9. So, everybody’s got a way of talking their own whatever their own book is. But 3.3 is definitely above what the Fed wants; that was the last number even if you use the core, which was 2.6. Obviously, that’s still too high. So the President had, when he did this, he knew affordability was on everyone’s minds and midterms are coming up. This is a tough sell at this point when everybody’s talking about affordability, especially if you don’t think that it was an imminent threat, which we’re hearing now from certain people. Well, I could give the other side of it, Joe. I don’t think it’s a tough sell to say that we just had a 0.2 at core, which rounded up to 0.2 from the ones. 2.6 is the lowest that core inflation has been since before Biden inflation started. And so we’ve made a huge amount of progress. And then remember, core excludes food and energy, but one of the things that was the best news of the CPI report is all the work that we’ve done to reduce the price of food. And so when I came in, the only thing everybody was talking about was the high price of eggs. Now egg prices are so low that farmers are actually complaining about it. Beef prices came down enormously because we opened up imports to beef. And so on, even drug prices have come down because of Trump RX. And so we’ve made a huge amount of progress on the individual items that affect inflation, and then we’re also, of course, addressing the macroeconomic issue of how you get inflation down, and that’s by being supply-side. So you’ve got to increase supply—that puts downward pressure on inflation—and cutting government spending, which went down, by about the deficit, by about $400 billion last year. I guess it’s possible that it takes a while, even if things do resolve in the Middle East. There are some people that think that oil could be permanently—not permanently, but at least higher for an extended period, or at least well above where it was. I think we had a five-handle, didn’t we, a print at one point below 60. So you think 80 is the new normal and that’s going to make it—no, you don’t. No, no I don’t. I think that if you go ahead and again, the exact timing month to month is something that the futures markets are making guesses about. But if you look ahead, then the price of oil has got to at the end of this go down, and it’s got to go down because of all the production in the US that we’re doing because of our energy dominance strategy, and all the production that’s going to come online in the Middle East once the straits are open and that risk premium is gone. And think about this in terms of how long it’s going to take to recover, the Saudis still have about 2 million barrels a day of excess capacity. They can turn the knobs and increase that production and put it in boats and ship it around the global economy as soon as the strait is open, they can get going on that. And so my view is that I think there’s been energy production that’s been suppressed by the Iranian behavior in the Middle East and that when that takes off, the long-run oil price is going to go down, down, down. Now, if you’re a person who’s an oil producer right now and you’re expecting lower prices, say a year from now or two years from now, then you should turn the spigot up as much as you can right now because you’re going to get higher prices now than you will a year or two from now. And I think that’s one of the offsetting factors that we’re seeing right now; people are eager to get in and sell their stuff at this temporarily high price. Hey Kevin, curious your reaction to this story in the Wall Street Journal about the UAE contacting Scott Bessent about potentially the United States providing some kind of currency swap or bailout or line of credit or rescue—I don’t know what—if the strait remains closed and it continues to injure their economy. What do you make of that and what do you know about that? Well, I haven’t talked directly with Secretary Bessent about this, but as you know, the UAE has been an incredibly valuable ally throughout this effort and I’m sure the Treasury Secretary will make every effort to help them out should that be necessary. But right now, President Trump has said he thinks the talks are moving forward very positively. He said that the talks will continue and even into this evening in his Truth over the weekend. And so I think that it probably won’t be necessary, but again these are very close allies. Secretary Bessent has shown a nimbleness and a cleverness about helping our allies when they’re in time of need. Argentina, that finally worked out, right? Oh sure it did, the US made money. That’s right, the US made money. What’s going to happen with—how does this finally work with—be glad it’s not you, because Tom Tillis would be not, you know, if it was the other Kevin, you wouldn’t be having a hearing either. How does it finally work out, do you think? Well first of all, I’m really thrilled that my old friend Kevin Warsh is the nominee. I’m very happy to be here and I can tell you that he’s an incredibly strong nominee. He worked at the National Economic Council when he was a kid. He went to the Fed and was the youngest Governor. He was there during the financial crisis. He’s got impeccable credentials and so Congress should or the Senate should confirm him as soon as possible. My expectation is that after everybody sees him in his hearing and sees how deft on his feet he is, how knowledgeable about the Fed he is, and how good his ideas are about returning the Fed towards a place where it’s non-partisan, that it’s going to be hard to resist voting yes. Right, but how do we get there with the—I don’t know whether you call it a roadblock, but both sides are talking at each other. The President hears that Tom Tillis isn't going to do it, and then he doubles down on the subpoenas, and Janine Pirro sends people over to the construction site. I mean, how is this finally going to work out? J Powell’s going to stay on the Fed till 2100, till the year 2100. It just seems like there’s a lot of talking at each other at this point, and Warsh is the one who’s caught in the middle of it. Well, I don’t think he’s caught in the middle, I think he is the middle, and that’s where he wants to be. And the bottom line is I think after the hearing, people will see what a strong candidate he is. And don’t forget that in the end, Kevin Warsh is going to be the Fed chair. And when he’s Fed chair, then everybody who wants to find out about all the cost overruns and everything else that’s been going on over the last couple of years will of course be able to do so because we’ve got a Fed chair there who people trust.
