With international trade, companies are the ones out there importing and exporting. When governments suddenly impose tariffs, companies are the ones at the front lines dealing with the practical consequences. When Soumaya Keynes and I were working on our book last year, we interviewed executives at these companies, both big and small, from all over the world. We were curious. When hit with tariffs, why couldn't they just immediately move their supply chains out of China? Again, when hit with tariffs, when would they raise prices to their customers and when would they pass along cost increases to their suppliers? Because there were so many tariffs, we had so many questions. I'm really excited to get back into these questions and to visit with one very special company on this week's show. Oh, and the book is titled "How to Win a Trade War." It will be out in May, but you can pre-order "How to Win a Trade War" today from anywhere that you buy books. And with that, let's get into the show. You are listening to an episode of Trade Talks, a podcast about the economics of trade and policy. I'm your host, Chad Bown, the Reginald Jones Senior Fellow at the Peterson Institute for International Economics in Washington. In this episode, we're going to tell the story of one very small and very special American manufacturing company and how it coped with President Trump's tariffs in 2025. We are in Fall River, Massachusetts. Fall River is an old manufacturing town with a very rich history of manufacturing textiles in America. And this building is where we make the chair cushions, a very simple but necessary product for folks across the country. What are we looking at here? People are finishing off the chair pad, stuffing it into the shell. Lots of different fabrics. This is the fill. This is so if you have like a decorative pillow or a cushion that's thicker, it's not that sort of two-inch slab-type shape. It's not made out of that foam. It's filled with like a fill that gets blown in and then sewn shut. I'm Sam Cooper. I'm co-owner and Senior Vice President of Business Development for Clearview Corporation. This is a three-generation family company founded 60 years ago by my grandfather. My brother and I are running it now. Our main customers are Walmart, Kohl's, Meijer, you know, in the Midwest, Amazon online, you know, Wayfair—really anywhere you'd go for home goods. Home textiles and kitchen textiles really are the departments it is in. Clearview makes chair cushions in Massachusetts. I grew up in a small town not too far away in Connecticut. A couple of towns away from me was the Hitchcock Chair Company. Hitchcock made beautiful wooden chairs for the kitchen and dining room. They were gorgeous, but so uncomfortable to sit in, especially through a really long Thanksgiving dinner at Grandma's house. All of these inputs are combined somehow into one of your chair cushions. How does Clearview make those cushions and where do you get all of these inputs from? So we buy upholstery fabric from textile mills, mainly from China. So the fabric gets shipped in from China. We sew a shell. Specifically, we make a chair cushion called The Gripper. This is an innovation my father came up with in the 90s. I'm not a man of hyperbole, but I would say it revolutionized the chair cushion industry because the cushion has no ties, so you don't have to tie it to your kitchen chair. Instead, there's a sort of gripper material on the bottom of it built into the chair cushion, and it stays on there. So that material comes from the United States. We definitely could have used The Gripper cushion at Grandma's Thanksgiving. The Gripper chair cushion is our main product, by far our biggest seller; probably over 80% of our total sales is the Gripper chair cushion. So these are all cutting machines. When we get the sheets of gripper material, you get stacks of them, and it cuts it into the shape from like a CAD file. And then you have that, and then you sew it to the fabric, stuff the fill into the cushion, and sew it shut. The fabric comes largely from China, though some of it we do source domestically. We sew the shell and then we stuff it with largely polyester fill and sew it closed, box it up, and ship it out to retailers. We'll post pictures of these Gripper seat cushions on our social media feeds. But tell us more about what goes into the cushion. What's most important about the inputs Clearview needs to make The Gripper so popular? Let's start with the fabric. It's very durable. A lot of people are using these for their chairs in a kitchen. They want to be able to clean them. They're being used every day, being used by children spilling spaghetti sauce or whatnot on it. And so they need to really last. So you need that really sturdy fabric. Upholstery fabric, a lot of it is made in China. They have real expertise in textiles, of making all sorts of different fabrics. Customers want a lot of different looks and feels and textures. China's very good at doing things like a chenille or a different kind of woven pattern. And they're very quick and efficient. My father and my uncle worked with these mills in their early days when they were getting into this business, and they're able to deliver consistently and at a very competitive price, which allows us to be competitive enough to sell to Walmart. And then I should also say we do source some domestically. There are still some upholstery fabric mills, a number in North Carolina. There's a more limited selection of what's left of that production in the US, but it is still important for us to have domestic production. We try to support that, you know, when we can. Why China? Why are they such an important source for these fabrics? I think it's just the amount of selection that you can have there. There's a lot of clustering, I feel, there with different specialties. And the specialties go just all the way kind of down the chain of how that fabric's made, from the different dyes to the yarns. There's a lot that actually goes into fabric, believe it or not, even though it looks like a very simple product. And all of those specialties are there and they're very deep. If they can't do something, they can talk to someone who can. You know, my family's been going to China for many years and visiting with our factory partners there, going to various trade shows. My brother and I went last year. We're at the Canton Fair. You know, you have these large booths with all the various American retailers named, showing their customer list. Have you tried working with textile mills in other countries? Over the years, we definitely have worked with mills in other countries. We've bought some fabric there. The specialties aren't as specific to a lot of the styles that our customers want. So in India, like, they don't specialize in chenilles as much. We have purchased fabric from Vietnam and, you know, that fabric's been fine. It's just really the amount of selection and specialty. And you know, I can say I think in some other countries that there's been more delays, more kind of loose ends; things don't show up as you thought they would. Let's talk about tariffs, but let's start by going back to the first Trump administration. How did the tariffs that President Trump imposed on China back in 2018 and in 2019 affect your company? As a company that imports raw goods, dealing with customs and dealing with HTS codes, dealing with products that have different duties have always been, you know, a part of the business. That certainly changed when you had the addition of the Section 301 tariffs, which tariffed different types of fabric. That was a big focus of some of the 301 tariffs where, again, we can come back to chenille. I think chenille was tariffed less than other types of fabric, which is part of the reason why you'll probably find it in a lot of home textiles or furniture. I believe it's chenille, you know, under the 301, is tariffed at a 7% rate and other fabrics are tariffed at a 25% rate. So if you bring in a container, you know, that's a really substantial difference in how that fabric is essentially taxed, right? And what you can offer to a customer. And how did those Section 301 tariffs from the first Trump administration affect what manufacturing jobs would be done where? One thing that's really kind of perplexed my brother and I and been very frustrating in conversations we've had is, in fact, a lot of raw material is in fact tariffed higher than fully made products. A lot of the fabric is tariffed under the 301. Some of it is tariffed at like 25%. But if you get fabric cut into shells that can then be stuffed and sewn shut, that is tariffed less if it's already cut, just similar to a finished product. Our process is we buy rolled fabric in, we cut it into the shapes of the chair cushion, and then, you know, sew it with the gripper material and fill it. But getting hit with tariffs on those materials that are higher than the tariffs on a cushion fully made in China can work against Clearview creating more jobs. So we're shipping in raw materials here to then make into cushions and I think, you know, the fully made cushions are tariffed at a lower rate. That, to me, just doesn't make sense. Well, if you're tariffing just the raw fabric at a higher rate than you're tariffing that shell that's already been maybe 75% closed, well we're losing that cutting and even sewing job here in the US. So again, maybe you could have more people sewing stuff here if that wasn't the case. So that was the first Trump administration. Donald Trump was elected president again in November 2024. At that stage, what did you expect to happen? Back after Trump was elected, my brother and I were talking like, "Okay, you know, this is going to change." Tariffs were a big part of the campaign. I think we took the president at his word that this is something they're going to focus on. So certainly it was something we were expecting on some level, but you don't really know. We go to a fabric show in High Point, North Carolina, twice a year. It's Interwoven in High Point. You meet with upholstery suppliers. And so, you know, tried to meet with folks from Vietnam, meet with folks who are sourcing fabric from India, Turkey. You know, where else can we kind of look for other sourcing? Again, you know, working with our domestic partners too, of course. And just seeing what those prices are. At the end of the day, even at that point, it's kind of hard when, you know, you could maybe buy some fabric from Turkey but it's triple the price. Did you bring in a bunch of additional inventory of fabric at that stage? No, you know, I think we didn't particularly front-load any inventory more than I think we would usually; maybe a few items but not particularly. Again, that's really dangerous too, right? Bringing on too much inventory—that could tank the business in the other way. It's obviously a large cash outlay upfront before you've received any money from your customers. It's an issue because what if it doesn't sell? You know, or what if you don't sell through it and then you're stuck with rolls and rolls of fabric that you paid for and you're having to sell in a close-out or a discount or otherwise find someone to take it or recycle it. Also, number three is a key point there: that's storage. You know, we have space, but not unlimited space. So that brings us to January of 2025 and Trump's inauguration. The president imposed his first new tariffs on China right away in February, but under a very different law from his first administration, and the tariffs escalated from there. How did Clearview respond? I remember the first 10% were like, "Ooh, all right," you know, like, "Where's that coming from? That's not, again, like the process under the first go-around of this administration." You know, that was very sudden. "Okay, 10%, that's damaging but not kind of catastrophic." Another 10%, "Okay, like, what do we have on the water that's coming in? How much is that going to affect us?" You were hit with 10% tariffs in February, another 10% in March, and then suddenly in April, President Trump increased tariffs on China by another 125%. How did you react to that? That was bananas. The 125% was totally wild. I mean, that is effectively a trade embargo, right? One, you kind of hope that the nightmare will end soon, you'll wake up from it. You know, us as makers of the humble chair pad, yeah, we do not have the loudest voice, but there are a lot of other folks that are affected by this. In fact, just about everyone around the world, right, is affected by something like that. You know, I think we looked again like, what's on the water? We did hold up shipments, things that were in the process of being made, saying "Stop, do not send it to the port," you know, like, if it comes in and it's double the price, that will be damaging. We did look at like, okay, what if we have to pay this on one or two containers? You know, okay, we can kind of weather that. But if it goes on much longer, then that's a real problem. At what point do we need to ask for a price increase from our customers and start having that conversation? So you did have to call some of your suppliers in China and say, "Please do not send us anything right now." Yeah, yeah, we called suppliers and said "Hold. Do not finish production until further notice." Just all of that was very disruptive, extremely disruptive. Did that have any ripple effects later on? Certainly some of the delays in shipping. We weren't late with any shipments or anything like that, but there were certainly some points where we were holding less inventory, walking a little bit closer to the line of when the deadline is to get product to the distributors and customers and things like that. Certainly the timing definitely disrupts our normal cadence of bringing in inventory and going through a schedule. And then just, you know, I think there was an effect on sales. I think a lot of people kind of froze. There was definitely a dip in sales. How far in advance are orders for seat cushions by your customers—these big retailers like Walmart and Wayfair—usually placed? The buying is done sometimes 18 months, 24 months in advance, the products they've picked. Changing a product out at a national store is a big deal. It goes through testing, it goes through labeling, there's a lot of evaluation. There's the store plans out what products go there. So just swapping something at a large retailer is not a decision, it's not something one that I think can be done on a wholesale level, you know, overnight for sure. You mentioned earlier conversations with other fabric suppliers in Vietnam, Turkey, India, as well as North Carolina in the United States. How do you switch over to new suppliers? What you can't do is just swap. A lot of people are like, "Oh, can you just like get this made in somewhere else?" No, you can't. They don't make that fabric. It would take them years to develop it. In the US, it would take years to do it. In some places they won't do it at all. So even as something as simple as this product—which in the grand scheme of things compared to, you know, electronics or chips or whatever, you know, even though it's pretty straightforward—it takes really years and years and maybe decades of building up certain specialties. The switch can't be flipped there. And I suppose even if you could swap suppliers, it's not like there were only tariffs on China the way there were back in 2018 and 2019. Trump put those Liberation Day tariffs in April 2025 on everyone. And later in the year, he hit countries like India with even more tariffs that added up to 50%. How did all of that affect your thinking about alternative suppliers? Yeah, we restarted some conversations with some suppliers in India, with suppliers in Vietnam, sending different samples, different products, with the understanding that this wasn't going to happen overnight but just again, you know, seeing what else is out there. But yeah, so once that 50% tariff hit, I was like, "Oh, okay." You know, like, no country's immune to this tariff shock. In a way, it was almost like, "Okay, need to do what we do in the way we do it best and kind of just deal with the tariffs as they come because there's no gaming it." Okay, these tariffs go on and swapping into new suppliers outside of China to get around the tariffs is not an option, in part because President Trump has now imposed tariffs on everyone's fabrics. So you go back to your existing suppliers in China. Tell us about those conversations. So you know, we went to some of our suppliers. We're like, "Hey, you're reading the news. We're all reading the news. We see what's going on. You know, this is going to sting when it hits the shores of the US. So can you split it with us? Can you give us a little discount?" And some of them did. You know, did take off a little bit. Certainly when it got to 145%, we're talking about like we're getting single-digit decreases, not multi-digit decreases in the cost. So, so yeah, it's a very small percentage of it. But why couldn't your Chinese suppliers lower their fabric prices when you went back to them? Policymakers, especially President Trump, have said repeatedly that foreigners like these suppliers are ultimately going to pay these tariffs by lowering their prices to keep selling into the US market to companies like Clearview. But it sounds like that was not your experience. How would you explain to those policymakers why Chinese suppliers can't simply lower their prices? I'd explain it, you know, like it's any other business. You know, they're making goods, they have a bottom line, they can't take a loss, they're trying to make a profit. There's kind of simple math involved. Certainly like any partnership in a customer-vendor relationship, you can always work with people on price. But again, there's a limit, and they don't want to go below that limit any more than we do. Obviously, the US is a major customer, but again, you know, people can only sell stuff at a loss and they do sell to other countries. And when I was speaking actually with a lot of suppliers when I was at the Canton Fair last year, some of them said, "Yeah, we stopped selling to the US market, you know, with a lot of the uncertainty because we were trying to focus on, you know, other regions." Okay, you went to your fabric suppliers and asked but only got a little discount from them. Next, you go to your customers to ask them if you can raise the price that you charge these retailers for them to put your seat cushion on the shelves in their stores. These are Walmart and Kohl's, obviously multinational retailers that are giant companies, especially compared to Clearview with your 50-something employees. What were those conversations with your customers like? So asking for a price increase from a customer is never a conversation you're really looking forward to, right? No one wants to increase prices on customers. We don't want the end customer to have to pay more. Again though, we have to do the math. Again, being a domestic manufacturer, which is a rarity in this business, we held off as long as possible, you know, long after the announcements. We didn't want to be the first person to have to have that conversation with any retailer at all. Look, we've been supplying some of these large-box stores for decades. You know, a big part of that is I think we do offer a very competitive price. I'm sure there is always somebody, right, who could probably do something cheaper. Would it be the exact same? Probably not. But it's a very, very competitive industry. It's a very low-margin industry. So a big part of that is our stability, is shipping on time, is again being domestic here as an advantage, using that advantage to be flexible, very low minimum orders and all of those things. So, you know, as far as we can be as a good supplier and a good vendor partner, that's what we want to emphasize. So again, by the time we reach out to them, it's not like this is something that's unique to our company that makes us the problem child. When did you finally have to raise prices? You know, after the several rounds of tariff increases—the, you know, first round of 10%, the second round of 10%, the Liberation Day tariffs where we're at triple digits—we did have a conversation with some of our customers to get a modest price increase. Again, emphasizing that we can absorb some of it being a domestic manufacturer, but our inputs are being taxed very heavily. Let me play devil's advocate for a moment. The counterargument would be something like, because President Trump put tariffs on everything this time—not just the fabric but also the finished cushions coming in from other countries like China—that your retail customers would have to pay higher prices for cushions even if they wanted to switch and buy them from someone else. So you shouldn't need to worry about losing business because all cushions out there were going to now be more expensive, right? You know, look, everyone's got a wallet. There's so much money in it. We're delighted that they'd like to buy our chair cushions. Hope we provide them an excellent product. Hope we make their sitting experience a little bit more comfortable every day. But also understand people have mouths to feed. People have insurance to pay. So certainly very sympathetic that, you know, you can't just pay more for something and that takes from something else. Let's talk about the direct impact of the tariffs on your business and your workers. Before all these recent tariffs—so back at the beginning of 2025—how was the home furnishings industry doing overall? In COVID, the home goods business was very good. You know, people are at their homes, they're ordering things online, and anybody with really anything in the home will tell you this: that there was a great rush of buying stuff. And, you know, chair cushions are a cheap way to refresh a room, but even furniture, all things. And then of course, you know, COVID ends, people want to get out of their homes, understandably. Of course, there's inflation, you know, all these different factors, and a lot of sales in the home goods industry have been really hit hard over the past several years. So entering then, you know, the industry is starting from a place of, you know, not great. So when the tariffs hit, we weren't expanding; we weren't on an expansion mode. But when the tariffs hit, you know, I think yeah, like you definitely pause any sort of investment that you're going to make. And what about your workers? The last thing we ever want to do is lay off workers, and you know, we didn't have to lay off workers. People have been working here longer than I've been alive, and this is a union factory. This is a place where, you know, we pay for folks' health care, you know, 401k. As a manufacturing job, you know, there's not a lot of these left in the area. So again, that volatility definitely didn't help things on that front. Shifts in the sort of shipping and all of this and chaos does then all of a sudden—yes, you do have to put in a weekend shift or an overtime shift. We had to have people, you know, working overtime on weekends and things like that to meet certain orders. What about contract manufacturing? You are an American manufacturer. After all of the tariffs went on and the US is now a high-tariff country for home furnishings, did any foreign companies come to you to ask if you and your workers could manufacture things for them in the United States? When the tariff was at its highest rate and even after, there were some Chinese firms saying, "Hey, we're a big supplier of certain types of cushions to retailers. You know, what if we sent you those shells and you guys stuff them and sew them here?" We did quote them a price. It was too high. Contract manufacturing is tough on that level. It's hard to do without very slim margins and then things can change. But even so, I think obviously labor and our costs here are much higher than theirs there. So there were certainly some calls on that level and we weren't able to, you know, take advantage of that opportunity just because of the dynamics of how it is to make things here. Now let's jump ahead to February 2026 and the Supreme Court. There had been this court case challenging the legality of President Trump imposing all these tariffs under the International Emergency Economic Powers Act—this IEEPA authority. Were you following the Supreme Court case? Absolutely following the Supreme Court case, watching it very closely, you know, from a couple of different levels. One, should IEEPA authority be struck down, that should at least create some more certainty or more runway in terms of how much tariffs change on a day-to-day level. If all of a sudden someone says you have to pay double for something—which is what happened, right, in April—someone says "I'm going to tax you on whatever you're buying" and you don't have those funds planned out for it, if you don't have alternatives, then yeah, that's a major shock. Obviously, there's still a very big commitment to tariffs, you know, that's been very clear. But Section 301 requires an investigation, there's a comment period, there's a lot more administration to it. It's not a tweet or whatever. And then, you know, we'll see about the refunds. I don't know. I did ask Sam about tariff refunds. He said that they had to hire an attorney to pursue the money that the Supreme Court has now said the US government owes to companies like Clearview. To him, it was just a lot of uncertainty. Clearview did hope to get some of that tariff revenue back that they paid last year, obviously, but at this stage, they just didn't know. Let's look ahead. The current tariff situation is that Clearview still faces the Section 301 tariffs President Trump imposed on China during his first administration, plus now the Section 122 tariffs of 10% he imposed on all countries after February's Supreme Court decision. The president has also announced new Section 301 investigations that could lead to more tariffs in a few months, but that is unclear. What's Clearview's reaction to this new environment? I guess this is a scenario we're familiar with, right? If the current situation stays like it is, whether it's the 10% or not 10%, then you know that we can live with. We'll see what comes out of the 301 process. That wouldn't be great if that went up. By no means are we saying we are okay with whatever happened in the last 301 process, that those goods should have been tariffed. I think tariffs are a tool that, you know, should be used very selectively. It would be great if it went down or went away. Doesn't seem like that's probably going to happen. Short of that, we'll keep going. You know, it's one challenge among many others I would say, in terms of running a manufacturing business in the US. Being costs of energy, costs of all sorts of other things that come in—insurance, etc., etc.—that make running a business hard. Now there's new challenges, perhaps with, you know, world events. On those new challenges, I did also ask Sam about the Iran war. He mentioned two concerns that had popped up so far. First, there was a delay in getting some of the chemicals their US suppliers needed to make the Gripper for their seat cushions. He was worried that that might be related to the war. The glitch was quickly resolved, but to Sam, it highlighted the challenge of knowing about your supply chain vulnerabilities. He never used to have to worry about where the company that made his cushions' grippers sourced their chemicals. Now he does. Second, some of his Chinese suppliers talked about needing to raise their prices for his inputs. He worried that their higher costs, maybe because of higher energy prices in China due to the war, meant that that glitch might not be as quickly resolved. I wanted to end my time at Clearview on a more optimistic note. I asked Sam whether anything positive for his business had come out of all those tariffs in 2025. I do think there's been some more conversations with retailers. You know, our customer partners and everything have always appreciated that we produce domestically. Nothing's quite materialized yet, but perhaps there's been a perspective that's sort of more open to us supplying things that, you know, we wouldn't have been as competitive on. Sam, as the owner of a small American manufacturing company, if you had one big takeaway for US policymakers more than a year into all these tariffs, what would it be? At the end of the day, I think—and a lot of manufacturers would say the same thing—just taxing inputs that are being used for jobs here, to me, seems very short-sighted. Let's think about what manufacturing jobs we want, what jobs are the future. I'm glad, you know, we can still support these jobs here. Let's make it easy for the manufacturers that are still here to keep those jobs here, and trying to switch overnight just seesawing tariffs is not going to help those manufacturers. To wrap up this episode, I wanted to highlight five things I learned about the impact of tariffs on a small business. First, small tariffs are something a company can deal with. But make the tariffs high enough—like 125% in April of 2025—and companies will stop shipping. That can be hugely disruptive if that means your inputs are suddenly cut off. Second, there are many reasons why it was hard for Clearview to immediately switch to new suppliers. Sometimes those alternative suppliers simply did not exist. But even if they did, it would take time to qualify them for what Clearview's demanding retail customers required. Third, if you're a small manufacturing company responsible for paying the tariff bill, it is hard to pass on the cost of that tariff increase to your input suppliers or your retail customers. Fourth, putting tariffs on foreign-made inputs makes it even more difficult for American manufacturers to compete and to create American jobs. And fifth, the suddenness of the 2025 IEEPA tariffs was really stressful for small companies. They may not have a lot of cash lying around to suddenly pay double for the inputs that they need. At least the Section 301 tariffs, as bad as those are, have a process which allows companies to plan and adjust for what may be coming next. And that is all for Trade Talks.
